REPORTS OF Libraries Sub-Committees. 194 REPORT OF THE CLAPHAM PUBLIC LIBRARY SUB.COMMITTEE, For the Year ended 31st March, 1912. The Sub.Committee have pleasure in presenting the Twenty. IFifth Annual Report on the work of the Clapham Library. The number of books issued during the year was 140,527 in the Lending Library and 22,615 in the Reference Library, being a total issue of 163,142 volumes, and a daily average of 674 volumes for the 242 days on which the library was open. The number of books now in stock is 18,571, of which 14,798 are in the Lending Library and 3,773 in the Reference Library. Advantage has been taken of the compilation of a new general subject.catalogue of the Lending Library to re.arrange the books in the various classes, and to discard a number of obsolete and worn.out works. 4,183 readers' ordinary tickets (including renewals) and 1,388 students' tickets were issued during the year in the Lending Library. In order to allow of further provision for show.cases, and better accommodation for serving readers, the counters in the Lending Library have been altered under the superintendence of the Borough Engineer. Collapsible gates have also been erected for shutting off the Lending Library (when closed) from the other departments of the institution. The Sub.Committee desire to express their cordial thanks to the donors of various books, periodicals, etc. The Borough Council, in November, 1911, re.appointed the Mayor (Alderman Dawnay, J.P.), Alderman Lidiard, and Councillors Bulcraig, J.P., Cox, Cundall, Duncan, Henderson and Young 195 as the Sub.Committee for the Clapham Library for 1912. Councillor Cundall was re.elected Chairman, with a vote of thanks for his services during the previous year. Several changes have occurred on the staff during the year. Mr. A. Wheatley, caretaker for 22 years, was superannuated, his place being filled by the appointment of Mr. G. Rye. Messrs. H. V. Hind and H. Hetherington, assistants, left to take up other municipal positions, and C. W. Stephens and H. J. Wheeler were engaged as junior assistants. In conclusion the Sub.Committee have great pleasure in bearing testimony to the efficiency of the Librarian and his staff and the general satisfactory working of the Library. On behalf of the Sub.Committee, JAS. W. CUNDALL, Chairman. 6th May, 1912. 196 REPORT OF THE PUTNEY LIBRARY SUB.COMMITTEE, For the Year ended 31st March, 1912. 1 The Sub.Committee have pleasure in presenting the Twenty. Fourth Annual Report of the Library. In December, 1911, Councillors Hoile, Jackson, Mathias, Radford and Rawson were appointed by the Borough Council to act as Sub.Committee for the year 1912. Councillor Radford was elected Chairman for the year. A new scheme for the issue of catalogues has been adopted. It is intended to issue a new edition every second year. The first issue under the new scheme was made last year, and was so well received that the edition was exhausted by the end of the year. A bust of the late Sir George Newnes, Bart., the donor of the Library, has been presented to the Library. A committee of local gentlemen, presided over by His Worship the Mayor, Alderman Dawnay, J.P., secured the necessary funds. The bust was unveiled by Sir William White, K.C.B., and formally handed over by him on May 23rd, 1911, to His Worship the Mayor, who accepted it on behalf of the Borough. With a view to economy the list of newspapers, etc., has been curtailed and a reduction of £10 has been made in the estimates for the ensuing year. The Annual Estimates for the year amounted to £1,173. the estimated product of a penny rate in the Putney Ward, plus receipts from the sale of catalogues, etc. The decision of the Sub.Committee to keep the Lending Department open on Thursdays has been appreciated, and good use has been made of the department on that day. The Sub.Committee desire to thank the donors of various books and newspapers to the Library. »97 Lending Department. The stock in the Lending Department has increased by 816 volumes ; of these 798 were acquired by purchase and 18 were presented. Two hundred and sixty. seven volumes were withdrawn from stock, and 360 worn.out books were replaced, making a total of 13,464 volumes now available for the use of borrowers. The issues have increased by 9,337 volumes, the total number being 84,980, as compared with 75,643 issued during the year 1910.11. On March 31st, 2,443 borrowers' tickets and 346 students' tickets were available for use in the department. The sum of £115 os. iod. was received from the sale of catalogues, fines, etc., during the year. Reference Department. The Reference Library is shelved in open cases, a plan greatly appreciated by readers. During the year 24 volumes have been added to the stock, making the total 2,450 volumes. Magazine Room. Thirty.nine monthly magazines and reviews, seven quarterlies and 55 weekly periodicals are placed in these rooms, which continue to be used by an increasing number of readers. The magazines and reviews are transferred to the Lending Department when replaced by new issues, and are greatly in demand for home reading. News Room. The list of newspapers, etc., provided in this room includes 32 daily newspapers, among which are Birmingham, Cardiff, Dublin, Edinburgh, Liverpool, Manchester and Plymouth daily journals, 53 weekly newspapers and periodicals, and 57 monthly and quarterly publications. A hearty vote of thanks was accorded to Mr. A. W. Gosden for his services as Chairman of the Sub.Committee during the year. The Sub.Committee again have much pleasure in recording their satisfaction with the work of the Librarian and his Staff. Signed on behalf of the Putney Library Sub.Committee, J. C. RADFORD, Chairman. 198 REPORT OF THE STREATHAM, BALHAM AND TOOTING LIBRARIES SUB.COMMITTEE, For the Year ended 31ST March, 1912. The Sub.Committee have pleasure in submitting their report on the work of the Streatham, Balham and Tooting Libraries for the year ended 31st March, 1912. The followirg members of the Borough Council were appointed, in November last, the Sub.Committee for 1911.12 : Councillor T. W. Watson (Chairman), the Mayor (Alderman A. D. Dawnay, J.P.), Alderman W. J. Hewett, J.P., Councillors the Rev. J. H. Anderson, M.A., T. J. Cox, J. Garrett, J. T. Hallinan, C. Kingston, and A. Douglas Robinson, M.A., LL.B. Books Issued. The number of books issued at the three libraries was 487,013, an increase of 685 on last year's return. The circulation at each library was as follows :— Li bra r Lending Library. For Reference purposes. Total. Average Daily Issue. The Tate Library 191,861 10,791 202,652 841 Balham Library 166,928 6,169 173,097 718 Tooting Library 108,812 2,452 111,264 461 Total Number of Volumes issued 487,013 2,020 Particulars of the number of books issued in the various classes of literature will be found tabulated on page 205. The figures given above for the Streatham and Balham Libraries respectively are the highest totals that have been recorded since their establishment. The small decrease which has occurred at the Tooting Library is confined entirely to works of fiction, the issues in the other classes of literature having slightly increased. 199 Borrowers' Tickets. Borrowers' The number of Borrowers' Tickets issued Tickets. during the year was as follows :— No. of Tickets issued. Library. Ordinary Tickets. Students' Tickets. Total. The Tate Library 5,371 2,335 7,706 Balham Library 4,135 563 4,698 Tooting Library 2,721 1,177 3.898 Total 12,227 4,075 16,302 The large number of Students' Tickets issued (especially at the Tate Library) is an indication that this additional privilege continues to be much appreciated by borrowers. Students attending the local classes of the London Workers' Educational Association have been granted the same facilities for pursuing their studies as those afforded to students attending the University Extension Lectures. Stock. Every year the selection of books for the libraries necessitates increasing care in seeing that important works are not overlooked, careful consideration being also given to the provision of suitable books bearing on the numerous and varied subjects of general interest occupying public attention. The extensive use which is made of the libraries necessarily involves every year a considerable outlay for the replacement of populai works that constant use has rendered unfit for further circulation. The stock at the 31st March, after making the necessary deductions on account of replacements and discarded books, numbered 46,596 volumes, located as follows :— No. of Volumes. The Tate Library, Streatham 22,357 Balham Public Library 14,007 Tooting Public Library 10,232 Total 46,596 200 More detailed information respecting the stock is given in the general table on page 206. At the annual stocktaking in July the number of books which could not be accounted for was four. 3.930 volumes were re.bound during the year. The constant attention given by the staff to the repair of books tends largely to keep down the expense of bookbinding. Sir William J. Lancaster, J.P., has again manifested his kind interest in the Tooting Library by presenting a copy of the " Victoria History of Middlesex " to the Reference Department. The cataloguing work at the libraries has been specially heavy during the past year. A new Subject.Catalogue, in dictionary form, of the books in the Tooting Library was published in September. The revision and renewal of cards in the card catalogues and the re.cataloguing of the stock at the Tate Library have also demanded close attention. The monthly lists of additions have been posted regularly at each library. Cataloguing. Cash Receipts. Cash The following is a statement of the receipts Receipts. from fines, sale of catalogues, etc., at the three libraries :— £ s. d. The Tate Library, Streatham 138 17 6 Balham Public Library 89 18 0 Tooting Public Library 64 6 4 Total £293 1 10 The amount paid by borrowers to replace lost or damaged books was £9 is. nd. Reading Rooms. A list of the newspapers and periodicals supplied to the Reading Rooms is given on pages 207.216. The list'of papers purchased for the libraries was revised by the Sub.Committee in January. 201 The Reading Rooms continue to be largely attended at all times of the year, and the selection of periodical literature provided appears to meet with general satisfaction. The rooms are open on Sundays (except during the months of July, August and September) from 3 to 9 p.m. The work of painting the exterior wood and iron work of the Balham Library and sundry repairs at the Tooting Library received attention during the year. The difficulties of administration arising from the present insufficient accommodation at the Tate Library have for some time past occupied the attention of the Sub.Committee. Unfortunately, the Libraries Committee are unable at present to recommend the Council to sanction the small loan that would be necessary to carry out the Sub.Committee's proposals for enlarging the Lending Department and providing the public with facilities for consulting books of reference. Signed on behalf of the Sub.Committee, THOS. W. WATSON, Chairman. 6th May, 1912. 202 REPORT OF THE WANDSWORTH LIBRARIES SUB.COMMITTEE. For the Year ended 31st March, 1912. The Committee appointed by the Council in November, 1911, were Councillor Harrison (Chairman), the Mayor, Councillors Couzens, Everett, B.A., Evershed, Junr., J. A. Robinson and Shillington. A vote of thanks was passed to Councillor W. J. Harrison for his services as Chairman during last year. Issues. The efforts of the Sub.Committee have been specially devoted to leading users of the Library into the reading of literature which is calculated to improve and instruct as well as providing for them interesting and entertaining books for leisure hours. The results are very satisfactory in that the proportion of educative issues has gone up, whilst that of fiction has gone down, and it is hoped to see further advances in the same direction. The gross issues are higher (see tabulated statement). Coronation. The Libraries were closed for the Coronation event on June 22nd and 23rd. Annual Closing. It was decided that no change be made in the date of the annual closing of the three libraries in Wandsworth. Maintenance. West Hill Library. The linoleum at the West Hill Library, after being down since 1885, has been renewed at a cost «f £23. Reference Room. The facilities for readers are so deficient and the impossibility of bringing all the book stock of this class into one place is so apparent, that the Committee can only in the absence of the means to rectify the trouble, put the facts on 203 record in the hope that one day soon this disadvantage may be removed. No plan of the library buildings at West Hill being in existence, it was considered advisable to have one prepared by the Borough Engineer. Plan. Catalogue. Allfarthing Lane Library. Catalogue of the books at Allfarthing Lane Library was ordered to be printed. Painting and Decorating. In accordance with the terms of the lease the work inside and out at this branch was carried out at a cost of £60. To avoid disturbance to readers in the newsroom the chairs have been fitted with noiseless rubber pads. Garratt Lane Library. The issues have gone up very considerably and the space at our disposal there for stock is becoming so inadequate that steps must be taken at an early date to provide proper accommodation if the needs of the district are to be even approximately met. General. Expenditure. The decision of the Libraries Committee to allocate the library rate on a different basis enabled this Sub.Committee to increase its book purchases. Donations. Various donations of books, etc., were made during the year, and suitable acknowledgments sent to the donors. Cash Receipts. Fines, etc., amounting to £104 10s. gd. were received. Staff. An insurance policy has been taken out to relieve the Council of compensation responsibility in the event of accident to any member of the staff. During the year Mr. S. Bastin was elected to fill a vacancy in the Rating Department; Mr. A. Freeman was promoted, and his place filled 204 by Mr. R. G. Austin, who afterwards passed the 2nd division civil service examination and on leaving was replaced by Mr. R. T. Mayrick. Mr. T. Cooper, porter, West Hill Library, has retired on superannuation allowance, his place being filled by Mr. J. A. Bright. Mr. J. Hawes, porter, Garratt Lane Library, was replaced by Mr. J. Phillips. The attention of the Librarian and his Assistants at the three libraries was all that could be desired, and they are thanked for same. Signed on behalf of the Wandsworth Libraries Sub-Committee, J. A. ROBINSON, Chairman. Ist May, 1912. 205 CLASSIFICATION AND NUMBER OF BOOKS ISSUED. Clapham Library. The Newnes Library, Putney. Streatham and Tooting. Wandsworth. 149 Zymotic Diseases, Death-rate per 1,000, 1901-1911 51 RE PORT of the Medical Officer of Health for the Year 1911. To the Mayor, Aldermen and Councillors of the Metropolitan Borough of Wandsworth. Sir and Gentlemen, I have the honour to present the Annual Report on the health and sanitary condition of the Borough for the year 1911. Population. From a return which has been furnished by the Registrar General it is possible to give the exact population enumerated at the Census taken on the 2nd April, 1911. From 1905 to 1910 the population has been, for the purpose of this Report, estimated by dividing the number of inhabitants at the Census of 1901 by the number of rated hereditaments in that year, and then multiplying the number of rated hereditaments in the middle of each year by the factor thus obtained. This method has given fallacious results, due to the fact that there has been a great decrease in the number of persons per rated hereditament as compared with 1901. 2 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The Registrar General, in his preliminary report, states that the estimations of the population of London based on the number of inhabited tenements have been framed for the London (Equalization of Rates) Act, 1894, and that the total error in the population of London is only half that by the old method, but that one of the most noticeable features was in the case of Wandsworth, which was over-estimated by nearly 10 per cent., while it was nearly correct by the old method. He further says: "These instances serve to show that the best devised methods for estimating populations of local areas cannot be considered trustworthy in the later years of a decennium, even under the most favourable circumstances. "They, therefore, emphasize the importance of more frequent Census enumerations, not only in the interest of vital statistics and sanitary progress, but also for the purpose of ensuring the proper distribution of public funds when this is to be determined by the number of the population." The following Table shows the Census population for each of the Wards for 1901 and 1911, with the percentage increase in each Ward:— Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 3 TABLE I. Ward. Males. An examination of the above shows that the increase in the different Wards has varied considerably, being as low as 33 per cent. in Clapham North, and as high as 1183 per cent. in Tooting. The total increase for the Borough was 79,368 persons, 36,980 males and 42,388 females, or 343 per cent. This was the largest increase in the Metropolitan Area, the next being Lewisham with 26.2 per cent. The increase in the Borough is not so great as in the decennium 1891-1901, when it was 49.2 per cent. For the whole of London the population was 4,522, 961, compared with 4,536,267 in 1901, a decrease of 3 per cent. Table I. also shows that the number of males increased by 35.6 per cent. and the number of females by 33 per cent. In the subdistricts the percentage increase in the number of males was less than of females in Putney and Springfield, while in the other subdistricts this percentage was higher. Clapham North 28,772 5,081 566 28,868 5,704 5.06 „ South 22,589 4,115 5.48 29,728 6,829 4.35 Putney 24,139 4,553 5.30 28,246 6,119 4,61 Streatham 33,624 6,759 4.97 49.192 11,756 4,18 Balham 38.034 7.204 5.27 47,009 10,333 4.55 Tooting 16.473 3,341 4.93 35,962 7,410 4.85 Southfield 21,924 3,963 5.53 34,607 7,385 4.68 Fairfield 18,520 3,896 4.75 20,604 4,087 5.04 Springfield 27.959 4,384 6.39 37,186 7,016 530 Whole Borough 232,034 43,296 5.35 311,402 66,639 4.67 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 5 A comparison of the number of persons per rated hereditament for the years 1901 and 1911 shows that there has been a considerable alteration during the decennium, and this to a large extent explains why the calculation of population during the decennium has proved a fallacious one. The number of persons per rated hereditament has decreased in the whole Borough from 5.35 to 4.67, a diminution having occurred in all the sub-districts with the exception of Fairfield Ward, where there was a slight increase. The diminution was greatest in Clapham South, and Springfield, and least in Tooting. Prison 1,225 125 1.350 1,406 137 1,543 Middlesex County Asylum (part of) 507 735 1,242 496 684 1,180 Total 3.399 2,571 5.970 4.188 3.667 7,855 7 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The total population of the above Institutions was 7,855, an increase of 1,885 since 1901, the increase being largely due to the erection of Tooting Bec Asylum. As, however, a proportion of the inmates of the Footing Home, the Workhouse, and the Metropolitan Asylums Board's Hospitals belong to this Borough, a deduction of 5,189 persons, 3,164 males and 2,025 females, made from the uncorrected population gives approximately the corrected population, 308,156, compared with 300,061 in 1910, and 290,462 in 1909. As the complete Census returns will not be available till the close of 1912, no particulars can be given as to occupation or overcrowding. Births. During the 52 weeks ending the 30th December, 1911, 6,699 births, 3,365 of males and 3,334 of females, were registered, compared with 6,967 in 1910, 6,985 in 1909, 7,331 in 1908, and 7,036 in 1907. The total number of births registered in this Borough was 6,555, but 216 births occurred in Institutions outside the Borough of persons belonging to the Borough, and 72 births in Institutions in the Borough of persons not belonging to the Borough, making a corrected total of 6,699. Of the 216 births which were registered outside the Borough, with the exception of 10 all occurred in Poor-Law or Lying-in Institutions, 107 in Clapham Maternity, 47 in the General Lying-in Hospital, 24 in St. Thomas's Hospital, and 38 in various Institutions in other parts of London. The rate for the whole Borough is much lower than any previously recorded, being 1.48 per 1,000 under 1910, and 4 26 under the decennial average. The birth-rates for the year in all the sub-districts and in the Borough as a whole show a considerable reduction compared with the decennial average, while compared with the previous year there has been a reduction in all the sub-districts, especially in Tooting and Wandsworth, with the exception of Clapham, where there is a slight increase in this rate. The birth-rate for the whole of London was 25.0 per 1,000, compared with 25.39 in 1910 and 27.55 the decennial average, while for England and Wales the rate was 24.4 per 1,000, compared with 25.05 in 1910, and 27.20 the decennial average. The proportion of births to the number of possible mothers, or the total number of females living in the Borough at ages 15 to 45 years, was, in 1908, 89.5, in 1909, 82.4, in 1910, 80.1, and in 1911, 73.1. In London it was, in 1910, 86.3, and in 1911, —; and in England and Wales, in 1909, the last available return, 102.3. The legitimate birth-rate, calculated on the married female population, was, in 1901, 215 per 1,000, and, in 1911, 180; while for London it was 186 in 1910, and in England and Wales 234 in 1901, and 209 in 1909. These rates will require correction when the detailed Census returns for 1911 are available. The Registrar General states that in the main the fall in the birth-rate is due to diminished fertility of married women, and that this diminished fertility is due, in some measure, probably to their greater average age, but largely no doubt to deliberate restriction of child bearing. 159 births occurred in the Workhouse during the year, and of these 13 belonged to Clapham, eight to Putney, 67 to Streatham, 14 to Tooting, 35 to Wandsworth, 62 to Battersea, and xo to other places. 10 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Of these 159 births 104 were illegitimate, nine belonging to Clapham, six to Putney, 13 to Streatham, eight to Tooting, 22 to Wandsworth, 37 to Battersea, and nine to other places. In the Borough, excluding the Workhouse, 111 illegitimate births occurred, 24 in Clapham, nine in Putney, 23 in Streatham, 23 in Tooting, and 32 in Wandsworth. Of the births registered outside the Borough, 11, four males and seven females, were illegitimate. One of these belonged to Clapham, one to Putney, three to Streatham, one to Tooting, and five to Wandsworth. The total number of illegitimate births was 180, 34 of which belonged to Clapham, 16 to Putney, 39 to Streatham, 32 to Tooting, and 59 to Wandsworth, compared with 185 in 1910, 175 in 1909, and 177 in 1908. The percentage of illegitimate births to total births was 2'68, compared with 2.65 in 1910. 2.50 in 1909, 2.41 in 1908, and 3.4 for the whole of London for 1910. During the year 4,607 births were notified, 836 from Clapham, 335 from Putney, 1,068 from Streatham, 590 from Tooting, and 1,778 from Wandsworth. 11 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 173 births were notified from the Workhouse, of which 99 belonged to this Borough, and 74 to other places. Of the 99 which belonged to this Borough, the home addresses of 18 were in Clapham, of eight in Putney, of 18 in Streatham, of 14 in Tooting, and of 41 in Wandsworth. The percentage of births notified, less still-births, to those registered was 70.3, while 207 per cent. were not notified, compared with 60.3 and 397 in 1910, showing an increase of 10 per cent. In the sub-districts these percentages were—In Clapham 70.3 and 207, compared with 61.1 and 38.9 in 1910; in Putney 66.8 and 33.2, compared with 50.0 and 50.0; in Streatham 60.1 and 39.9, compared with 55.1 and 44.9; in Tooting 60.5 and 395, compared with 61.2 and 38.8; and in Wandsworth 79'g and 20.1, compared with 65.6 and 34.4. A considerable increase has taken place in all the sub-districts, especially in Wandsworth. The proportion of still-births to total births was 2.2 per cent., compared with 1.7 per cent. in 1910, and with 2.2 per cent., the mean for the whole of London for the year 1910. The work carried out by the female Sanitary Inspectors in connection with the Notification of Births Act is given later on in this Report when dealing with Infantile Mortality. Deaths. During the 52 weeks ending the 30th December, 1911, 3,456 deaths, 1,615 of males and 1,841 of females, were registered, compared with 2,856 in 1910, 3,279 in 1909, 3,230 in 1908, and 3,337 in 1907. Compared with 1910 there has been an increase of 600 deaths, but a decrease of 281 compared with the decennial average corrected for increase of population. The uncorrected death-rate for the Borough was 11.02 02 per 1,000, compared with 8.16 in 1910, 11.08 in 1909. 11.24 in 1908, and 11.94 in 1907. The average uncorrected rate for the decennium 1901-1910 was 11.93, or .91 above the rate for the year 1911. 13 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. In the sub-districts these rates were as follows:—10.84 in Clapham, 8.64 in Putney, 878 in Streatham, 21.53 in Tooting and 10.09 in Wandsworth. The reason for the high rate in Tooting is that in that subdistrict there are a number of Public Institutions, as the Tooting Bec Asylum and the Tooting Home, where persons from other parts of London are received and among whom the mortality is very great. To ascertain the corrected death-rate corrections must be made to the above by the addition of the deaths in Out-lying Institutions and other places of persons belonging to the Borough, and the subtraction of all deaths in Public Institutions and other places in the Borough of persons not belonging thereto, whose domiciles were in other parts of London. The deaths occurring in other parts of England and Wales are for the first time included, as these are now received from the Registrar General. Only the deaths occurring in the first three quarters of the year are, however, available this year. The total number of deaths for the 52 weeks, corrected for both internal and external Institutions and other places was 3,776, compared with 3,105 in 1910, 3,520 in 1909, 3,380 in 1908, 3,416 in 1907, 3,461 in 1906, and 3,871 the decennial average corrected for increase of population. During the year 721 deaths occurred in Public Institutions and 129 in other places in the Borough. Of the total of 850 deaths, 669 were of persons not belonging and 181 of persons belonging to the Borough. Table X. shows the deaths in Public Institutions in the Borough of persons not belonging thereto, arranged as to ages, causes of death, and Institution in which the death occurred. The greatest number of deaths was due to Old Age, and of the 243 registered, 201 occurred in Tooting Bec Asylum, 23 in the Tooting Home, eight in the Middlesex County Asylum, and seven in other places. In the Middlesex County Asylum the greatest number of deaths occurred from diseases of the Nervous System, Tubercular disease of the Lungs, Old Age, disease of the Respiratory Organs, Bright's disease, Enteritis, and diseases of the Circulatory System. In the Tooting Bec Asylum the greatest number of deaths occurred from Old Age, diseases of the Nervous System, and diseases of the Respiratory Organs; in the Hostel of God from Tubercular disease of the Lungs and Cancer; and in the Tooting Home from Tubercular disease of the Lungs and Old Age. In the Fever Hospitals 17 deaths occurred from Scarlet Fever, compared with 10 in 1910, 43 from Diphtheria, compared with 15, 34 from Whooping Cough, seven from Measles, four from diseases of the Respiratory Organs, three from Tubercular disease, one from Simple Meningitis, and one from Cerebro-Spinal Fever. 16 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. All causes 989 549 440 157 4S 56 40 56 202 239 191 202 93 232 125 337 405 454 77 53 21 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The total number of deaths in out-lying Institutions was 989, 549 of males and 440 of females, compared with 812 in 1910, 897 in 1909, and 783 in 1908. 405 of these deaths occurred in Workhouses and Workhouse Infirmaries, compared with 395 in 1910; 454 in General ard Special Hospitals, compared with 317 in 1910; 77 in Public Lunatic or Imbecile Asylums, compared with 90 in 1910; and 53 in the Metropolitan Asylums Board's Hospitals, compared with 10 in 1910. Of the 454 deaths in General and Special Hospitals, 62 occurred in St. Thomas's Hospital, 43 in Bolingbroke Hospital, 19 in Belgrave Hospital, 34 in Victoria Hospital, 16 in St. George's Hospital, 16 in Westminster Hospital, eight in King's College Hospital, 21 in Guy's Hospital, 19 in Long Grove Asylum, 10 in Horton Asylum, 10 in Banstead Asylum, 10 in Charing Cross Hospital, nine in Cane Hill Asylum, seven in Middlesex Hospital, nine in West London Hospital, and seven in St. Bartholomew's Hospital. In the South Western Hospital of the Metropolitan Asylums Board, there were 24 deaths, one from Diphtheria, one from Enteric Fever, 21 from Measles, and one from other causes. In the Western Hospital there were 26 deaths, two from Enteric Fever, two from Whooping Cough, one from Diphtheria, one from Cerebro-spinal Fever, and 20 from Measles ; and in the Park Hospital three deaths, one from Measles, and two from other causes. The number of deaths in these and other Institutions outside the Borough is shown on Table LXXX. The corrected number of deaths after adding the deaths of persons belonging to the Borough and subtracting those of persons dying in the Borough and not belonging thereto, was 3,775, 1,833 of males and 1,943 of females, an increase of 670 compared with 1910, but a decrease of 96 compared with the corrected decennial average. 22 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The death-rate in England and Wales in 1911 was 14.6 per 1,000, compared with 13.4 in 1910, 14.5 in 1909, and 14.7 in 1908 ; in the 77 Great Towns 16.4, compared with 14.3 in 1910, 147 in 1909, and 14.9 in 1908; and in the 136 Smaller Towns 14.4, compared with 12.9, 13.9 and 14.0. If a comparison is to be made with other places it is necessary to again correct the corrected rate, viz. 12.25, for age and sex distribution. This is done by multiplying the rate by a factor obtained by dividing the standard death-rate in England and Wales at the Census of 1901, with the standard death-rate for the Borough at the same period, the standard death-rate at the Census 1911, not yet being available. This factor has been ascertained to be 1.0547, which gives a rate for this Borough for the year 1911 of 12.92 per 1,000, compared with 10.90 in 1910, 12.77 in 1909 and 12.62 in 1908. In the separate sub-districts of the Borough the corrected death-rate was 11.02 in Streatham, 11.88 in Putney, 11.73 in Tooting, 13.15 in Clapham, and 13.29 in Wandsworth. The rates in all the sub-districts were much higher than in 1910, due to causes which will be commented on in another part of this Report. Table XVI. shows the death-rate in the Borough, in the five sub-districts, and in England and Wales for the year, as well as the rates for the previous 10 years, compared with the decennial average. This Table has been calculated from the Census population of 1911, and shows more correctly than could be done in intercensal years the exact mortality in the various places. During the previous decennium the lowest mortality in all the sub-districts, as well as in London and England and Wales, was in the year 1910. The rate in 1911, although higher than in 1910, is still, in all the subdistricts with the exception of Streatham, lower than the average of the previous 10 years. Compared with London and with England and Wales the Borough rate is considerably lower. 27 Clapham. Putney. Streatham. Tooting. Wandsworth. Whole Borough. London. England and Wales. The first Chart shows the death-rate in the Borough for each fortnight, compared with the quinquennial average 1906-1910. The Chart shows that on 10 fortnights the rate was above that for the previous quinquennium, viz.:—on the fortnights ended the 25th February, nth March, 25th March, 8th April, 22nd April, 12th August, 26th August, 9th September, 23rd September, and 21st October. During the rest of the year the fortnightly rate was under the quinquennial rates. The highest rate, 17.28, occurred during the fortnight ending the 25th March, and this was due to the great number of deaths from Measles and Respiratory diseases; and the lowest rate during the fortnight ending the 29th July, when it was 7 92 per 1,000. The next Chart shows the Annual Death-rate per 1,000 during each week of the year for the whole of London and for each fortnight for the Borough of Wandsworth. During the year, with the exception of the four weeks of March, the rate in this Borough was under the rate for the whole of London. The highest rate in London was in the third week of August, and the lowest on the last week of June. Table XVII. shows the vital statistics of the whole Borough for the year and for the previous 10 years, as well as the average for the 10 years. This Table shows clearly the variations in the births and deaths, the birth and death-rates, and the infantile mortality for the decennium, compared with the year 1911. Compared with the decennial average there has been a considerable decrease in the birth-rate and a slight decrease in the death-rate, while the infantile mortality, which for the past 10 years had shown a most satisfactory decrease, now shows a considerable increase and was higher than it has been since the year 1906. It will also be seen that there has been an increase of 291 deaths of residents in Public Institutions outside the Borough, compared with the 10 years' average. The corrected death-rate, although higher than it has been since 1906, is still under the 10 years' average. The four Tables which follow are inserted by request of the Local Government Board. Table I. is arranged to show the gross births and deaths in the district, and the births and deaths belonging to it with the corresponding rates. The rates in column 7 are calculated on the gross population, and the rates in 5 and 13 on the nett population, i.e., the population obtained by deducting from the estimated gross population the average number of inmates not belonging to the district in Public Institutions. The population given in column 2 of this Table is the population after the above correction has been made. Table II. shows the number of cases of Infectious Diseases notified during the year at all ages and at certain age periods in the Borough and in the separate sub-districts, and also shows the number of cases removed to hospital from the whole Borough. Table III. is the general mortality Table for the whole Borough, showing the number of deaths at all ages, and at certain ages, arranged as to cause of death. It also shows the number of deaths in Public Institutions and other places in the district, both of residents and non-residents. Table IV. shows in detail the deaths of infants under one year of age from certain diseases, arranged in weekly age periods, the first four weeks, from one to three months, and for three monthly periods up to one year. 33 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The increase in the percentage of deaths under five years was due to the great increase in the number of deaths from Measles, Diarrhoea and Enteritis. Infantile Mortality. The total number of deaths of infants under one year was 823, compared with 545 in 1910, 601 in 1909, 720 in 1908, and 715 in 1907, and is 52 under the decennial average corrected for increase of population. Of these, 156 occurred in Clapham, 70 in Putney, 180 in Streatham, 116 in Tooting, and 301 in Wandsworth. The infantile mortality, or deaths under one year to 1,000 births was 122, compared with 78 in 1910, 86 in 1909, 98 in 1908, 101 in 1907, and 125 in 1906. This rate was 119 in Clapham, compared with 104 the decennial average; 145 in Putney, compared with 112; 99 in Streatham, compared with 96; 116 in Tooting, compared with 137, and 143 in Wandsworth, compared with ill. In the whole Borough the rate was 54 per 1,000 births above that for 1910, and 13 above the decennial average. The rate in 1911 was higher than it has been since 1906,'when it was 125 per 1,000, and in the 10 years previous to 1911 it has only been higher in 1901, 1902, 1904, and 1906. In all the sub-districts, with the exception of Tooting, there has been a marked increase in this rate compared with the decennial average, this increase having been greatest in Putney and Wandsworth. In Tooting the rate is lower by 21 than the decennial average, and it has only been lower during the decennium in that sub-district in the years 1909 and 1910. 39 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Generally speaking, compared with the last two years, the increase in the rate has been, in the sub-district of Clapham, due to the increase in the number of deaths from Measles, Whooping Cough, Diarrhoea, Enteritis, Premature Birth and Congenital Debility; in Putney, to Diarrhoea, Enteritis, Premature Birth and Congenital Debility; in Streatham, to Diarrhoea, Enteritis and Diseases of the Respiratory Organs; in Tooting, to Measles, Diarrhoea, and Enteritis; and in Wandsworth, to Measles, Whooping Cough, Diarrhoea and Enteritis. The total number of births was 6,699, of which 6,519 were legitimate and 180 illegitimate, and of deaths 823, of which 81 were illegitimate. From the notifiable infectious diseases there were four deaths, three from Diphtheria and one from Erysipelas. 41 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. From Measles there were 41 deaths, a considerable increase compared with previous years. 21 of the deaths occurred in the sub-district of Wandsworth, 10 in Putney, five in Clapham, four in Streatham, and one in Tooting. From Whooping Cough there were 29 deaths, from Diarrhoeal diseases 274, from Premature Birth 101, from other diseases of infancy 160, from Tuberculous diseases 23, from diseases of the Respiratory Organs 118, from Convulsions 23, from Syphilis nine, from Suffocation (overlying) three, and from other causes 23. Compared with 1910 there has been an increase in the number of deaths from Diarrhoea, Measles, other diseases of Infancy, and diseases of the Respiratory Organs. In all the deaths, with three exceptions, one in Clapham, one in Streatham, and one in Tooting, the cause of death was certified by a medical man or by a coroner after inquest. 29.5 of the deaths occurred in the first month, compared with 40 9 in 1910, and 37.4 in 1909; 437 in the first three months, compared with 59.4 and 54.4; and 65.9 in the first six months, compared with 74-5 and 73. The Table shows that 101 died from Premature Birth, of which 96 occurred under one month, and 146 from Congenital Debility and Malformations, compared with 110 in 1910, and 124 in 1909. Of the total deaths under one month, viz.:—243, 96 occurred from Premature Birth and 85 from Congenital Debility and Malformations, a percentage of 74.4, compared with 80 per cent, in 1910. From Diarrhoeal diseases only 10 deaths occurred under one . month. The greatest mortality from these diseases occurred at three to six months, and six to nine months. From Tuberculous diseases 23 deaths occurred, 16 from Tuberculous Meningitis, one from Tuberculous Peritonitis, and 42 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. six from other Tuberculous diseases, compared with 14 in 1910, 17 in 1909, and 26 in 1908; and 118 from diseases of the Respiratory Organs, compared with 108, 115 and 134. Five deaths were registered as due to Meningitis, but enquiries of the medical attendant showed that all these were due to simple Meningitis. Table XXVI. shows the proportion of deaths per 1,000 births from five groups of diseases for each week under one month, from one to three months and for each three months up to one year of age. Compared with 1910 the rate from common infectious diseases is higher, due to the increase in the number of deaths from Measles and Whooping Cough, this increase having been greatest in Tooting, and Wandsworth. From Diarrhœal diseases there was a very large increase, the rate having been four times greater than in 1910. From Premature Birth, Congenital Debility and Malformations the rate was only slightly higher than in 1910, and this was also the case from Tubercular disease and from other causes. From the Table it will be seen that the rate from common infectious diseases was highest at the ages nine to 12 months, from Diarrhoeal diseases at three to six months, from Premature Birth, etc., under one month, from Tuberculous diseases at nine to 12 months, and from other causes from three to six months. Table XXVII shows the infantile mortality per 1,000 births from all causes and from certain causes for the whole Borough and for each sub-district. From Premature Birth the rate was lowest in Tooting and highest in Wandsworth, from Developmental diseases highest in Putney and lowest in Streatham, from Diarrhœal diseases highest in Putney and lowest in Streatham, from Measles and Whooping Cough highest in Wandsworth and lowest in Streatham, from Respiratory diseases highest in Putney and lowest in Streatham, from Tuberculous diseases highest in Tooting and lowest in Streatham, and from other causes highest in Streatham and lowest in Clapham. The Table also shows the rates for certain groups of diseases compared with the five previous years. From Premature Birth, the notifiable diseases, Respiratory diseases, Accidental and other causes, there has been a reduction, and from Developmental diseases, Diarrhoea, Measles, Whooping Cough, and Tuberculous diseases there has been an increase. This increase is marked in all the sub-districts in the case of Diarrhoea. It was noted in the Report for 1910 that the infantile mortality rate was the lowest on record, but in 1911 there was a considerable increase, more especially in the sub-districts of Putney and Wandsworth. The climatic conditions were this year most favourable for Diarrhoea and digestive diseases of infants, and the deaths from Measles and Whooping Cough in Tooting and Wandsworth were also influenced by such conditions. In July, August, and September the air temperature was above the 65 year's average on 76 days out of 92, compared with only 15 days in 1910 and 27 days in 1909, when the Diarrhoea mortality was low, and in 1911 the rainfall was much under the average, rain falling on 22 days, compared with 3.7 in 1910 and 45 in 1909. In July and August very high temperatures were recorded, the mean daily temperature on the 9th August having been the highest ever recorded. 45 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. There is no doubt that these climatic conditions were the cause of the largely increased mortality from Diarrhoea and Enteritis in infants. This will be well seen on the Chart facing page 98. Sixteen of the deaths occurred in July, 148 in August, and 60 in September. The high rate in Putney was due to Developmental diseases and Diarrhoea, and in Wandsworth to Diarrhoea and Measles. It is satisfactory to note that in Tooting the mortality was 116, which although 24 per 1,000 higher than it was in 1910, is still five per 1,000 under the average for the previous five years. 46 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The total number of deaths from these diseases was 1,154, 218 in Clapham, 73 in Putney, 279 in Streatham, 165 in Tooting, and 419 in Wandsworth, an increase of 487 compared with 1910, and of 142 compared with the corrected decennial average. Compared with 1910 there has been an increase in the number of deaths from Enteric Fever, Diphtheria, Puerperal Fever, 49 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Erysipelas, Measles, Diarrhoea, Tuberculous diseases, and other infective diseases, and a decrease in the number of deaths from Scarlet Fever, Whooping Cough, and Influenza. Compared with the corrected decennial average there has been an increase in the number of deaths from Measles, Diarrhoea and other infective diseases, but a decrease in all the others. From all infective diseases the death-rate was 374 per 1,000, (3.69 in Clapham, 2.57 in Putney, 2.89 in Streatham, 4.84 in Tooting, and 4.65 in Wandsworth), compared with 2.23 in 1910, and 3.28 the decennial average. The rate from the principal infective diseases, viz.:—Smallpox, Measles, Scarlet Fever, Whooping Cough, Diphtheria, Enteric Fever, and Diarrhoea, was 2.26 per 1,000 (202 in Clapham, 1.41 in Putney, 1.55 in Streatham, 3.28 in Tooting, and 3.07 in Wandsworth), compared with 78 in 1910. The high rate from these diseases was due to the increase in the number of deaths from Measles and Diarrhoea. In out lying Institutions 316 deaths occurred from Infective diseases (60 belonging to Clapham, 27 to Putney, 64 to Streatham, 45 to Tooting, and 120 to Wandsworth), compared with 196 in 1910. Of these deaths 136 occurred in Workhouse Infirmaries, one from Enteric Fever, one from Diphtheria, two from Erysipelas, 66 from Tuberculous diseases, four from Puerperal Fever, one from Measles, three from Whooping Cough, 53 from Diarrhoea, and five from other infective diseases; 113 in General and Special Hospitals, two from Whooping Cough, two from Diphtheria, three from Influenza, 44 from Diarrhoea, 59 from Tuberculous diseases, and three from other infective diseases; 16 in Public Lunatic Asylums, 10 from Tuberculous diseases and six from Diarrhoea; and 51 in the Hospitals of the Metropolitan Asylums Board, two from Whooping Cough, two from Diphtheria, three from Enteric Fever, one from Diarrhoea, and one from Cerebro-spinal Fever. B 2 50 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. In Institutions in the Borough 140 deaths from infective diseases occurred of persons not belonging thereto, compared with 91 in 1910. During the year 1910, of 6,878 children whose births were registered, 3,857 or 56.1 per cent, were successfully vaccinated, compared with 60.9 per cent, in 1909, 61.3 per cent, in 1908, 66.5 per cent, in 1907, 71.05 per cent, in 1906, and 74.6 per cent, in 1905. This is again a decided decrease compared with the five previous years. Compared with 1909 there has been a decrease in the percentage of successfully vaccinated in all the sub-districts with the exception of Clapham. The greatest decrease occurred in Streatham, and the smallest in Putney, while in the whole Borough there has been a decrease of no less than 4.8 per cent. Compared with the decennial average there has been a decrease in all the sub-districts, the greatest decrease having been in Streatham, and the smallest in Putney. 427 infants died unvaccinated, a percentage of 6'2 compared with 5.9 in 1909, 67 in 1908, 6.6 in 1907, 8.1 in 1906, and 8.4 in 1905. Of the 2,594 infants remaining, 19 or 27 per cent, were stated to have been insusceptible to vaccination; 1,003 or 14.5 Per cent, in respect of whom certificates of conscientious objection were received; 405 or 5.8 per cent, had vaccination postponed by medical certificate, leaving 940 or 13.6 per cent, as removed, not to be traced, or otherwise unaccounted for; while 227 or 3.3 per cent, remained on the books as unvaccinated, compared with 13.3 per cent, as removed, etc., in 1909, 13.6 in 1908, 13.4 in 1907, 101 in 1906, and 77 in 1905; and 3.3 remaining as unvaccinated in 1909, 1.5 in 1908, 4.4 in 1907, 3.1 in 1906, and 3.1 in 1905. The low rate of successful vaccinations in Streatham is a disquieting fact, as well as the great increase in the number of certificates of conscientious objections received, not only in that sub-clistrict, but in the Borough generally. Fortunately, Small-pox was not introduced into the Borough during the year, but there is no doubt that if such does occur the spread of the disease will be difficult to arrest owing to the greater proportion of unprotected persons. 59 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The Medical Officer of the Local Government Board in his Report for 1910-11, in dealing with the question of the introduction of cases and the methods adopted to prevent these cases from causing an epidemic, makes the following remarks, "the success hitherto achieved, in many small outbreaks that have occurred, is remarkable; but the slowly diminishing protection of the population by vaccination is a necessary cause of disquietude for the future. This is especially the case in certain districts in which the proportion of unprotected children is becoming large." Scarlet Fever. 869 cases of Scarlet Fever were notified during the year, compared with 687 in 1910, 987 in 1909, and 1,232 in 1908. 170 of these were in Clapham, 49 in Putney, 219 in Streatham, 125 in Tooting, and 306 in Wandsworth. 725 or 83.5 per cent, of these cases were removed to the hospital where nine died, and 144 or 16.5 per cent, were treated at home with one death. The total number of deaths was 10, of which one was in Clapham, two in Putney, four in Streatham, two in Tooting, and one in Wandsworth. The death-rate per 1,000 population was .03, compared with .04 in 1910. The percentage of cases removed to hospital, viz.: 83.5, is slightly lower than in 1910, when it was 84 per cent. The case mortality was 1.15 per cent., compared with 1.77 in 1910, 1.42 in 1909, and 1.7 in 1908, and is the lowest on record. The mortality among cases removed to hospital was 1.24 per cent., compared with 1.6 in 1910, and among those treated at home .69 per cent., compared with .57 in 1909. The mortality among the home-treated cases was thus much lower than among those cases treated in hospital. The following Table shows the rate of notified cases per 1,000 persons living for each sub-district and for the whole Borough for the year and the three previous years, as well as the corresponding rates for the whole of London. The rate in Tooting was to some extent raised by the notification of cases from the Fever Hospitals. Scarlet Fever and Schools. The next Table shows the number of cases of Scarlet Fever for each month of the year among children in attendance at elementary and other schools for each sub-district, also the number for the whole Borough, the number of cases among persons not at school, the number attending schools outside the Borough, as well as the number of cases in which, as far as could be ascertained, the diagnosis of Scarlet Fever was incorrect. Of the total number of cases, viz.:—869, the diagnosis was incorrect in 50 or 57 per cent.; 488 or 56.2 per cent, were in attendance at schools in the Borough; 23 or 2.6 per cent, attended schools outside the Borough; while 308 or 35.5 per cent, did not attend school, being either above or under school age. The percentage of children attending school was as follows: in Clapham, 101 out of 170, or 59 per cent.; in Putney, 22 out of 49, or 45 per cent.; in Streatham, 118 out of 219, or 54 per cent.; in Tooting, 69 out of 99, or 68 per cent.; and in Wandsworth, 178 out of 306, or 58 per cent. The percentage of cases in the Borough in attendance at school was higher than in 1910, while in the sub-districts the percentage was higher in all with the exception of Streatham. The percentage in Tooting is calculated after deduction of the cases notified in Institutions in that sub-district. In Clapham the schools chiefly affected were Wirtemberg Street, in which 17 cases occurred, Cavendish Road with 14 cases, and Bonneville Road with 12 cases. Ten of the cases in Wirtemberg Street occurred in October. Considerable difficulty was experienced in ascertaining the cause of these cases, and it was not until the 14th of the month that a mild case was discovered, which in all probability was the cause of several of the other cases. This child had suffered from a slight sore throat about the 20th September, but it was not till desquamation was definite, about the14th October, that a diagnosis could be made. 64 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. In this school only two cases occurred in November, and none in December. The school was visited on the 16th October, and the names and addresses of all absentees obtained. Their homes were then visited so as to ascertain definitely the cause of absence and to secure at the earliest possible period the examination of the child by a medical man. In all cases this was obtained, and it was not till a medical certificate was sent to the school that the child was permitted to return. In all the schools in the Borough this method Was adopted, and although a considerable amount of extra work was thus created, yet in my opinion it was the means of lessening to a large extent the spread of the disease. In Putney, of the 22 cases, five were notified in October and nine in November, seven of these being in attendance at Hotham Road School, and five at private schools. In Streatham the schools chiefly affected were Eardley Road with 18 cases, and Welham Road with 14 cases, while 30 cases occurred among children attending private schools. In Tooting, Ensham Street with 21 cases, and Sellincourt Road with 17 cases were the schools chiefly affected. 14 of the 21 cases in Ensham Street School occurred during the last four months of the year. In Wandsworth, Swaffield Road with 30 cases, West Hill with 18 cases, the Wandle with 15 cases, and Brandlehow Road with 14 cases were the schools chiefly affected. The Medical Officer (Education Department) of the London County Council excluded 45 children from 19 schools during the year, chiefly in October, November and December, until a medical certificate was received to the effect that the child was free from infectious disease. It was necessary to carry out disinfection in only four of the affected schools during the year after Scarlet Fever, but in all these cases the disinfection was carried out without closing the schools. 65 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. All the sub-districts show an increase compared with the previous year The Chart (facing page 66) shows the number of cases notified during each week of the year. The maximum number of cases, viz. :—23, was notified in the second week of November, and the minimum, viz. :—one, in the fourth week of September. Although the number of cases of this disease was considerably larger than in 1910, yet it did not take an epidemic form owing to the means adopted for dealing with the cases in schools. During the months of October, November and December, when the cases suddenly increased in number, some anxiety was felt and extra precautions were taken to prevent the attendance at school of children suffering from sore throat. The occurrence of sore throat was reported to me by the teachers of the elementary schools, and I am confident that without the cordial co-operation extended to me by them it is quite possible that the disease might have assumed an epidemic and more virulent form. In Clapham, out of a total of no cases, 46 or 42 per cent, occurred in children attending school ; in Putney, of 34 cases, 17 or 50 per cent. ; in Streatham, of in cases, 55 or 50 per cent ; in Tooting, of 106 cases, 41 or 387 per cent. ; in Wandsworth, of 149 cases, 65 or 437 per cent. ; and in the whole Borough, of 510 cases, 224 were in attendance at schools in the Borough, and 12 at schools outside the Borough, a total of 236, or 46.2 per cent. Of the remaining cases, viz. :—274, in 42 or 8 per cent., the diagnosis was incorrect, and 232 or 45.5 per cent, were not in attendance at school. In Clapham the number of cases of Diphtheria in attendance at school was 46, the greatest number occurring in Haselrigge Road School and the next in Wirtemberg Street School. Five of the cases in Haselrigge Road occurred in October and three in August, while in the other schools in Clapham the cases occurred sporadically during the year. In Putney four cases occurred in Hotham Road and four in Walkers Place Schools, but in five months of the year in Putney no cases were notified among children attending school. In Streatham, Welham Road with eight cases and Broadwater Road with eight cases were the highest. Three of the cases in the former school occurred in July, and three in the latter school in February. The sub-district of Tooting was not so free from Diphtheria as in the previous year. 10 cases occurred in Franciscan Road, four of these in July. Ensham Street and Sellincourt Road Schools had seven cases each, but in neither school was any particular class-room involved. In Wandsworth there were 65 cases, and these occurred mainly in the last three months of the year. In five of the schools only one case occurred in each, in three two cases, in one three cases, in one four cases, in one five cases, in two six cases in each, in one eight cases, in one 13 cases, and eight cases occurred in the various private schools. 75 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The principal school affected in Wandsworth was Swaffield Road, where 13 cases occurred, five in October, and five in November. Three of the cases in October occurred in one house. Extended experience in dealing with small outbreaks, such as occurred in this last school, emphasises the urgent necessity of swabbing the throats of suspicious cases. In several instances no diagnosis could possibly have been made from clinical symptoms alone, and it was only after careful swabbing, not only of the throat but also of the nasal passages, that the carrier cases causing the extension of the disease could be discovered. Of the total number of cases notified 42 were incorrectly diagnosed, compared with 39 in 1910, leaving a total of 468 actual cases, compared with 245 in 1910, and 335 in 1909. No cases of Diphtheria were notified as having occurred in children attending the following elementary schools in the Borough :— In Clapham :— Aristotle Road. Bonneville Road. Christchurch. New Park Road. New Road. St. Mary's, Clapham Park Road. In Putney :— National School, Roehampton. In Streatham :— Christchurch. Hitherfield Road. Roman Catholic, Nightingale Square. St. Andrews, Polworth Road. In Wandsworth :— Roman Catholic, North Side, Wandsworth Common. St. Michael's. St. Thomas's. In all, 14 schools remained free from Diphtheria, compared with .24 in 1910. 76 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Of the total number of cases of Diphtheria, six or 1.2 per cent, occurred among children under one year, 146 or 28.6 per cent, among children from one to five years, 276 or 54.1 per cent, from five to 15 years, 43 or 8.4 per cent. from 15 to 25 years, and 39 or 77 per cent. from 25 to 65 years. Compared with the previous year there has been an increase at five to 15 years, and at 25 to 65 years. Of the 428 cases of Diphtheria removed to Hospital 42 were reported not to have suffered from Diphtheria. Return Cases of Scarlet Fever and Diphtheria. 52 cases of Scarlet Fever occurred after the return of cases from the hospitals of the Metropolitan Asylums Board, compared with 40 in 1910, 46 in 1909, 57 in 1908, and 41 in 1907. There were also two cases of Diphtheria, compared with onein 1910, four in 1909, 17 in 1908, and one in 1907. 12 of the return cases occurred in Clapham, two in Putney, eight in Streatham, eight in Tooting, and 22 in Wandsworth. One of the return cases of Diphtheria occurred in Clapham, and the other in Streatham. Clapham 1 Feb. 12th May 12th 89 May 18th 6 Streatham 1910 2 Dec. 27th Feb. 16th 51 Feb. 27th 11 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 79 Enteric Fever. 58 cases of Enteric Fever were notified during the year (13 from Clapham, three from Putney, 14 from Streatham, two from Tooting, and 24 from Wandsworth), compared with 54 in 1910, 66 in 1909, 60 in 1908, 70 in 1907, and 96 in 1906. 42 of the cases were removed to hospital where four died, and 16 were treated at home with five deaths. The total number of deaths was nine (three in Clapham, none in Putney, two in Streatham, none in Tooting, and four in Wandsworth), compared with eight in 1910, six in 1909, seven in 1908, 10 in 1907, and 15 in 1906. The number of deaths was one more than in 1910, but 6.4 under the decennial average. The proportion of cases notified per 1,000 of population was .18, compared with .18 in 1910, .22 in 1909, .21 in 1908, and with •22 for the whole of London. The corrected death-rate from Fever was .03 per 1,000, compared with .02 in 1910, .02 in 1909, and .02 in 1908, while the rate for the whole of London was .03, compared with .04 in 1910, .03 in 1909, and .04 in 1908. Of the 42 cases removed to hospital, eight or 19.04 per cent, were found not to be suffering from Enteric Fever, compared with 29 per cent. in 1910. The Chart (facing page 66), shows the number of cases notified during each week of the year. In 16 of the weeks during the year no cases were notified, one case each in 21 of the weeks, two cases each in nine of the weeks, three cases each in five of the weeks, and four cases in one of the weeks. The maximum number of cases, viz. :—Four, was. notified in the third week of November. 80 Report of the Medical Officer of Health Of the 58 cases notified 10 were not Enteric Fever, four occurred in H.M. Prison, leaving 44 cases in which special enquiries were made as to the probable cause of the disease. In eight of the cases the disease was probably contracted by eating shell-fish, in 15 it was contracted outside the Borough (four in Ramsgate, three in South Africa, two in Brighton, and one each in Paris, Leeds, Bognor, Devonport, Broadstairs, and Northampton), in one the infection was contracted from a previous case in the same house, in three probably from watercress, while in 17 no history of infection could be ascertained. With regard to the eight cases caused by shell-fish, two were from oysters, three from mussels, two from winkles, and one from cockles. In the cases contracted outside the Borough, 15 in number, it was impossible to ascertain the exact source of infection, but in four of the cases it was probably from infected shell-fish, making a total of 12 altogether in which shell-fish were suspected. One of the cases contracted outside the Borough was infected by a carrier case at Brighton. Of the total number of cases, viz. :—12, infected in all probability by eating shell-fish, two died, while in the case of the other seven deaths no definite history of infection could be ascertained. Of the 10 cases which were not Enteric Fever, two were fatal, one from Tubercular Meningitis, and the other from Peritonitis. Cases of Mistaken Diagnosis. In 102 cases (50 of Scarlet Fever, 42 of Diphtheria, nine of Enteric Fever, and one of Cerebro-spinal Fever) information was received from the Metropolitan Asylums Board's and other hospitals. that the patients were not suffering from the disease notified or any other notifiable disease, compared with 107 in 1910, 112 in 1909, and 110 in 1908. Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 81 Of the 1,199 cases removed to hospital, in 102 or 8.5 per cent. the diagnosis was incorrect, compared with 12.6 per cent. in 1910, 9.5 per cent. in 1909, and 6.9 per cent. in 1908. Cerebro-spinal Fever. Five cases of Cerebro-spinal Fever were notified during the year (one from Clapham, one from Streatham, one from Tooting, and two from Wandsworth), compared with five in 1910. All the cases died, three from Cerebro-spinal Meningitis, one from Posterior-basic Meningitis, and one from acute Miliary Tuberculosis. C 2 82 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. In London 101 cases of this disease were notified during the year, compared with 115 in 1910. 25 deaths were registered as having been caused by Meningitis (two in Clapham, two in Putney, six in Streatham, four in Tooting, and seven in Wandsworth), compared with 19 in 1910. Five of these were of infants under one year, three from one to two years, three from two to five years, and 14 over 5 years. As in previous years., inquiries were made from the medical attendant, and in all the cases, with the exception of one, due to Posterior-basic Meningitis, the disease was stated to have been Simple Meningitis. Bacteriological Examinations. During the year 624 bacteriological examinations were made at the Lister Institute, compared with 318 in 1910, 397 in 1909, 491 in 1908, 466 in 1907, 407 in 1906, 174 in 1905, 121 in 1904, 125 in 1903, 98 in 1902, and 66 in 1901. Negative 1 3 7 1 4 16 Positive 2 ... 4 .... 5 11 Totals 77 75 167 43 262 624 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 83 In 202 out of 597 examinations for the bacillus of Diphtheria the result was positive, and in 11 out of 27 examinations of blood in suspectcd cases of Enteric Fever, the Widal reaction was obtained. The great increase in the examinations for Diphtheria was due to the more general use of this means of diagnosis by the medical men in the Borough, as well as to the more rigid supervision of suspicious cases of sore throat reported from elementary schools. There is no doubt that these examinations were the means of checking outbreaks of Diphtheria which threatened several of the elementary schools during the year. Puerperal Fever. 24 cases of Puerperal Fever were notified, compared with 20 in 1910, 26 in 1909, 14 in 1908, 25 in 1907, and 25 in 1906. Three cases occurred in Clapham, two in Putney, three in Streatham, four in Tooting, and 12 in Wandsworth. Eight of the cases (one in Clapham, two in Streatham, one in Tooting, and four in Wandsworth), or 33.3 per cent. were fatal, compared with 25 per cent. in 1910, 38.4 per cent. in 1909, 42 in 1908, 40 in 1907, and 44 in 1906. 15 of the cases were removed to the Union Infirmary, where four died, and nine were treated at home with four deaths. The death-rate was .025 per 1,000, compared with .016 in 1910, and .034 in 1909. The case-rate per 1,000 births was 3.5 compared with 2.8 in 1910 and 3.7 in 1909, and for the whole of London 2.7, compared with 2.6. In four of the cases registered midwives only were employed, while six of the cases were miscarriages. 84 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Four nurses had free baths, and of the others, one came from Battersea, five were district or surgical nurses, and the remainder had baths at their own homes. In 19 cases the personal clothing of the nurse was disinfected. Erysipelas. 202 cases of Erysipelas were notified during the year and 10 deaths occurred. 42 of the cases and three of the deaths occurred in Clapham, 10 cases and no death in Putney, 42 cases and four deaths in Streatham, 22 cases and no death in Tooting, and 86 cases and three deaths in Wandsworth. In 1910 the number of cases notified was 182, and the deaths four. 45 of the cases were removed to the Union Infirmary, where two died. Measles. During the year 230 deaths, 122 of males and 108 of females, were due to Measles, compared with 44 in 1910, 108 in 1909, 45 in 1908, 66 in 1907, and 65 in 1906. 27 of the deaths occurred in Clapham, three in Putney, 52 in Streatham, 44 in Tooting, and 104 in Wandsworth. The number of deaths is 186 more than in 1910, and I37.1 above the corrected decennial average. 41 of the deaths were of infants under one year, 80 from one to two years, 76 from two to five years, 32 from five to 15 years, and one from 15 to 25 years ; 85.6 per cent, of the deaths having occurred under five years of age. The death-rate was .74 per 1,000, compared with .14 in 1910, .37 in 1909, .15 in 1908, .24 in 1907, and .24 in 1906. Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 85 The death-rate for the whole of London was .56 compared with .40 in 1910, for England and Wales .36, and for the 77 Great Towns .47 per 1,000. For the past 10 years Measles has been of a comparatively mild type, and with the exception of the years 1903, 1904, and 1909, the number of deaths did not exceed 100. At the close of 1910 it was observed that the number of cases was increasing, especially in Clapham and Putney. In the latter sub-district the outbreak did not become extensive, the total number of cases notified from schools amounting only to 74. In the other sub-districts, however, the number of cases increased rapidly and for the first four months of the year thesanitary staff was exceptionally busy in visiting the houses in which cases occurred, as will be seen from the statement of the number of these visits made. During the year 4,136 visits were made in cases of this disease (compared with only 957 in 1910), 571 in Clapham, 99 in Putney, 1,152 in Streatham, 781 in Tooting, and 1,593 in Wandsworth. Table XLVIII. shows the number of cases of notifiable and non-notifiable diseases notified by the teachers of the elementary schools in the Borough. In all, 6,785 cases were notified, compared with 4,617 in 1910, and 4,342 in 1909. 3,617 of these were cases of Measles, 591 of Chicken-pox, 565 of Whooping Cough, 674 of Mumps, and 412 of Ringworm. There was a large increase in the number of cases of Measles, and a decrease in the number of cases of Chicken-pox, Whooping Cough, Mumps and Ringworm. With regard to Measles, 534 cases were notified from schools in Clapham, 74 in Putney, 983 in Streatham, 573 in Tooting, and 1,453 in Wandsworth. 86 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The schools chiefly affected were, in Clapham, Bonneville Rood, Cavendish Road and Telferscot Road ; in Putney, Hotham Road ; in Streatham, Mitcham Lane, Smallwood Road, and' Eardley Road; in Tooting, Franciscan Road, Ensham Street, Sellincourt Road and Fountain Road ; and in Wandsworth, Swaffield Road, Waldron Road, Elliott Central, Wandle, Garratt Lane and Earlsfield. Very few of the schools were free from the disease, ard from information received from private individuals and medical men it was ascertained that in most of the private schools cases had occurred. The means adopted for the prevention of the spread of the disease were exclusion from school, education of the parents of children where cases had occurred by means of the pamphlet published in last year's Report, and disinfection of the premises after cases had recovered. As far as could be ascertained from the notifications received from the elementary schools, the disease first showed in epidemic form in the month of January in Telferscot Road School, and next in Cavendish Road School. These schools, although in Clapham, yet draw a large proportion of their children from Balham. The disease then spread to Tooting and Wandsworth, next to Streatham, Putney remaining comparatively free. The Education Department of the London County Council were at the same time informed by the teachers, and Table XLIX. shuws the schools in which it was thought necessary to exclude infants in attendance. It will be seen from this Table, as well as from the Table of the number of disinfections carried out, that the disease was most prevalent in February and March, and as in these months the climatic conditions are such as to favour the occurrence of the more fatal complications of Measles, the case mortality was exceptionally heavy. In nearly all the cases Bronchitis or Pneumonia was given as the secondary cause of death. 87 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. TABLE XL VIII. School. Diseases. Scarlet Fever. Diphtheria. The total number of premises disinfected was 2,638 (303 in Clapham, 59 in Putney, 711 in Streatham, 418 in Tooting, and 1,147 in Wandsworth), compared with 418 in 1910, 1,012 in 1909, 884 in 1908, 955 in 1907, and 902 in 1906. The greatest number of disinfections was carried out in March, this being due to outbreaks in Clapham, Streatham, Tooting and Wandsworth. The greatest number of cases occurred in February, March and April. In August, September, October, November and December the number of disinfections was small as there were very few cases in any part of the Borough. 93 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The death-rate was .21 per 1,000, compared with .22 in 1910, .23 in 1909, and .23 for the whole of London in 1911. This disease was slightly less fatal in London and in this Borough in the year 1911 than in the two previous years. In the sub-district of Wandsworth it was more prevalent than in the other sub-districts The number of cases notified from schools was 565, compared with 919 in 1910, 538 in 1909, and 612 in 1908, 232 of these being from schools in the sub-district of Wandsworth, the schools chiefly affected in that sub-district being The Wandle and Earlsfield, and Streatham, where the schools chiefly affected were Mitcham Lane and Hitherfield Road. 94 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. There were also 70 cases notified from schools in Clapham. the schools chiefly affected having been New Park Road and St. Andrew's Street. In Putney the school chiefly affected was All Saints', and in Tooting, Franciscan Road and Tooting Graveney. The greatest mortality from Whooping Cough occurs among children under five years of age. Of the 66 deaths that occurred from this disease, 64 or 97 per cent. were deaths of children under five years. The pamphlet, published in last year's Report, was left at every house where a case of Whooping Cough had been notified from school, and also in all other known cases. During the year 446 visits were made by the Sanitary Inspectors in cases of Whooping Cough, 37 in Clapham, 3S in Putney, 121 in Streatham, 91 in Tooting, and 159 in Wandsworth. Other Diseases Notified from Schools. In addition to the 3,617 cases of Measles and 565 of Whooping Cough notified by the teachers of elementary schools, there were also notified 591 cases of Chicken-pox, 674 of Mumps, 412 of Ringworm, 76 of German Measles, 68 of inflammation of eyes, 52 of suspicious sore throat, and 118 of other diseases. No action is at present taken by the Sanitary Authority after notification of these diseases, although all the cases of suspicious sore throats were visited, so as to exclude the possibility of any of the cases being Diphtheria. In one of the schools in Clapham several cases of Impetigo occurred, and these were excluded until they had recovered. Chicken-pox was most prevalent in Streatham and Wandsworth, Mumps in Clapham and Streatham, and Ringworm in Streatham and Wandsworth. Epidemic Influenza. 24 deaths, 12 of males and 12 of females, were due to this disease, compared with 55 in 1910, 89 in 1909, 82 in 1908, 88 in 1907, and 70 in 1906. There has thus been the large decrease of 31 deaths compared with the previous year, and also compared with the corrected decennial average. Four of the deaths occurred in Clapham, one in Putney, 10 in Streatham, one in Tooting, and eight in Wandsworth. The death-rate was .08 per 1,000, compared with .18 in 1910, .30 in 1909, .28 in 1908, .32 in 1907, and .26 in 1906. 10 of the deaths were of persons from 25 to 65 years, and 10 of persons over 65 years. Diarrhoea. 350 deaths, 173 of males and 177 of females, occurred from all forms of Diarrhoea and Enteritis during the year, compared with 88 in 1910, 120 in 1909, 183 in 1908, 127 in 1907, and 307 in 1906. There has thus been an increase of 262 in the number of deaths from these diseases compared with the previous year, and of 218 compared with the corrected decennial average. 67 of the deaths occurred in Clapham, 28 in Putney, 79 in Streatham, 53 in Tooting, and 123 in Wandsworth. In all the sub-districts there was a very large increase compared with 1910 and previous years. The death-rate was 1.13 per 1,000, compared with .30 in 1910, .41 in 1909, .64 in 1908, .46 in 1907, and 1.15 in 1906. The total number of deaths of children under two years was 300, equal to a rate of .97 per 1,000, while the death-rate for the whole of London under two years was 1.17 per 1,000. The heavy mortality from this disease was not confined to this Borough, as will be seen from these rates. 97 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The death-rate in the sub-districts was as follows:—In Clapham 1.13, compared with .29 in 1910; in Putney .98, compared with .32; in Streatham .8o, compared with .17; in Tooting 1.53, com pared with .37; and in Wandsworth 1.36, compared with .38. Of the total number of deaths registered, 274 or 78 per cent. were of infants under one year, compared with 75 per cent. in 1910, 72 per cent. in 1909, 74 per cent. in 1908, and 78 per cent. in 1907.
