In the Chart which is this year inserted, the number of deaths and their relation to temperature and rainfall is graphically shown. It will be seen from these that in 1909 and 1910, when the temperature was below the average of the last 65 years in the three months, July, August and September, and when the rainfall was above the average, the number of deaths was small, whereas in 1911, when the temperature was much above the average and the rainfall below the average, the number of deaths from Diarrhoea and Enteritis was large. In the Registrar General's Report it was stated that the heat of the month of August was without precedent in the records of the country, that with a few exceptions the rainfall was everywhere deficient, and that a great excess of sunshine was noticeable practically everywhere. In the month of September the Report states that the first 12 days of the month were marked by three touches of unusual warmth, and this is well seen in the Chart. The rainfall also in London was low, and bright sunshine was in excess of the average. The Chart shows that the greatest number of deaths occurred in August, and that on only one day during that month no death occurred. It can also be seen that only three deaths from Diarrhoea occurred in July 1909, three in July 1910, and 14 in July 1911; 15 in August 1909, eight in August 1910, and 130 in August 1911; 27 in September 1909, 14 in September 1910, and 53 in September 1911. These climatic conditions were most favourable to fly-life, and from inquiries made in August and September it was found that the house fly was present in very great numbers in all the sub-districts. During the three months frequent inspections Diarrhœa No. of Deaths. Temperature and Rainfall. July. August and September, 1909,1910 and 1911. = Mean Temperature for 65 Years. 99 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. were made of stables and places where horse manure was kept so as to secure its frequent removal, but the prevalence of flies will not be much lessened in hot weather until the public are better educated in the methods that should be used for the storage of house refuse. Uncovered dust bins and the strewing of house refuse on back yards are still common, and in hot weather this refuse is more suitable for the development of flies owing to the fact that fires are not so much used, the amount of ashes produced being lessened and the amount of decomposable organic matter increased. The more frequent removal of house refuse by the Saritary Authority in the months of July, August and September, is most desirable, and is a subject that might with advantage to the public health be considered by the Council. The whole tendency of modern sanitation has been in the past to insist on the removal of effete products more quickly. Human excreta and slop water are removed at once by the water carriage system. The time will I trust soon come when the public will insist on the removal of other waste products, such as stable manure, excreta of other animals, as well as house refuse and decomposing stable and trade refuse, by a method as rapid and as satisfactory. Another point noted in the inspection of houses in the localities where Diarrhoea was most prevalent was the necessity of having back yards paved with impervious material. In the large majority of cases these yards are not utilised as gardens, but as dumping places for all sorts of refuse, and as suitable spots for the erection of small wooden sheds for keeping domestic animals. Great difficulty is experienced in dealing with the question of keeping domestic animals and I have personally made a considerable number of inspections for the purpose of ascertaining whether these are kept so as to be a nuisance. As will be seen in a later part of this Report a very large amount of work has been done in having yards properly paved, and in doirg away with nuisances arising from the accumulations of decomposing refuse and the keeping of domestic animals, but here also the education of the public is essential. 99 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. were made of stables and places where horse manure was kept so as to secure its frequent removal, but the prevalence of flies will not be much lessened in hot weather until the public are better educated in the methods that should be used for the storage of house refuse. Of the 256 cases inquired into, 31 were prematurely bom, and 17 were twin children, 13 had intestinal trouble from birth, nine were suffering from Rickets and Wasting, seven had Bronchitis and Pneumonia, four had Whooping Cough, seven had Congenital Defects, and seven had other diseases previous to the onset of the Diarrhcea. Two of the cases who were entirely breast fed probably contracted the disease from the mother while in another case a member of the family had been removed to hospital suffering from Enteric Fever when the infant was four days old. An inquest was necessary in one case and the father was committed to prison for six weeks for neglect. Of the 256 deaths, 54 occurred in Institutions outside the Borough. Herewith we submit to you the report of our work among infants for the past year. "We had the help of nine voluntary workers, but only five worked for any length of time, and we have been able with their assistance to visit among the poorer families in each Ward of the Borough, details of which are shown in the subjoined Tables. "The total number of visits paid, including re-visits and ineffectual visits was 6,974, the ineffectual visits totalling 1,526, so many having removed previous to the second visit. "The number of ineffectual calls was highest in Southfield, the reason being that many of the young mothers take their children out as much as possible. "The total number of visits paid after notification and registration of births will be found in Table LIV., which also shows the method of feeding at the first visit, and the number of ineffectual calls. "The Notification of Births Act, which came into force in this Borough in November, 1909, has enabled us to visit infants earlier than formerly. "Nearly all the births in Springfield Ward were attended by midwives, and the notifications in this Ward were very nearly as numerous as the births registered. "150 of the infants visited died during the year, their ages in months at death being shown in Table LVI. "It will be noticed that the heaviest mortality occurred in Springfield, where the deaths from Diarrhoea and Enteritis alone numbered 19. The previous year also the deaths in this Ward were the most numerous, then the causes were mainly Bronchitis and Pneumonia. 104 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. "The majority of the deaths occur in the streets and roads adjoining the Wandle, in 1911 especially the houses were infested with flies throughout the greater part of the year. "86 deaths occurred among infants previously visited by us in 1911, as compared with 19 in the previous year, this shows a percentage of 33.5 as compared with 29 .8. "The next most fatal complaints were Bronchitis and Pneumonia, 22 irfants dying of these, and six of Whooping Cough. 10 infants died of Marasmus, and seven of Congenital Debility and Inanition, five died of Convulsions, four of Tubercular Meningitis, two were suffocated, and two were premature. There was one death from each of the following diseases, Anæmia, Jaundice, Hydrocephalus, Congenital Syphilis, and Heart Failure, while the death of one was registered as caused by Debility from birth, Excessive Heat, the child being taken ill and dying the same day. "Of the 3,518 irfants visited, 127 were premature, and of these 17 died, and 46 were delicate from birth, 19 of whom died, six of these being twins. *• "We have found it exceedingly difficult to trace the illegitimate births registered, especially in the case of the Workhouse births where the address of the house at which the expectant mother had lodged for a short time before entering the Workhouse is often given, or in other cases, the address of the home, or of a member of the family who repudiates any connection with the mother, and assures us that she will not return to that address on leaving the Workhouse. Five wrong addresses were giver, 13 removed almost immediately after confinement, (two of these came here for confinement only), and 10 irfants we found had died previous to our first visit. "No information could be obtained about five infants, and 28 were put to nurse immediately after birth, while five of thoseborn in the Workhouse remained there. 105 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. "Of the 117 illegitimate infants visited, 27 were removed and could not be traced as the particular guardians had removed when re-visited, while 16 mothers came into the Borough only to be confined, the majority of these cases being in Tooting. Of those mothers from whom we could not get any information, 31 were not natives of this Borough. "At the first visit 69 infants were wholly breast-fed, but of the 48 re-visited only 12 were so fed. "20 of those visited died, 10 from Diarrhoea and Enteritis, three from Marasmus, three from Inanitior, three from Pneumoria, and two from Whooping Cough. "Table LV. shows the number of re-visits, and the method of feeding then found, the average age at the second visit being from four to five months. "The proportion of infants entirely breast-fed on the first visit is somewhat higher than in the previous year, being 81.6 per cent, as against 79.8 per cent, in 1910. The number of those having milk or food in addition is very nearly the same, the proportion of those being fed on breast and cows' milk showing a percentage of 43, this being the same as 1910, those fed on cows' milk alone were 30 per cent, as compared with 33 per cent, in 1910, and those fed on condensed milk 15 per cent., compared with 17 per cent, in 1910. "On our second visit we again found a slight increase in the proportion of those wholly breast-fed, the percentage being 63, compared with 62 in 1910. We also noted a decrease in the use of farinaceous foods, while the proportion of those fed on cows' milk and condensed milk is almost the same as in 1910. "The pamphlet that we distribute containing advice on the feeding of infants has been much appreciated by many mothers, in some instances we have found it has been treasured from the first year in which we visited the mother, and was being used for the second or third baby. 321 mothers told us that they were following the instructions contained in it . 106 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. "We found in the case of infants that were being fed by hand that 664 mothers were using the boat shaped bottles. Both boat and tube bottles were in use in 30 instances, and in 59 cases the infant was fed by a spoon. "On our second visit 32 tube bottles had beer discarded and boat bottles were then being used. "In our talks with the mothers, we have specially pointed out to them the dangers arising from the use of "comforters" or "dummies." We were glad to find that a great many mothers were aware of these dangers and were not giving the dummy to the child, but in many instances it is looked on as the universal pacifier, or as the medium by which the baby takes its medicine. We have often found a child of two or three years still sucking at a dummy and refusing to give up the use of it. "1,075 mothers were wise enough not to begin the use of the 'comforter,' but 2,443 were giving it, though more than half of this number assured us 'it was only occasionally given.' In 253 instances the mothers had discontinued its use altogether on our second visit. "With regard to the employment of midwives, out of 3,518 mothers visited 1,215 availed themselves of their services. Of these 1,170 employed midwives registered under the London County Council and 45 had non-registered midwives. Any cases of infringement of the Midwives Act discovered by us we have at once reported to you. The sanitary defects found in the course of our work were remedied, or if requiring structural work, referred to you. "138 intimation notices were served, and five statutory notices were required." io7 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. TABLE LIV. INFANTS VISITED. Clapham, North. Clapham, South. Putney. In all 3,518 infants were visited, compared with 3,448 in 1910, and 2,269 in 1909. It is satisfactory to observe that with the assistance of the voluntary workers, infants were visited in all the Wards of the Borough. The total number of deaths among those visited was 150, 18 in Clapham, 20 in Putney, 21 in Streatham, 21 in Tooting, and 70 in Wandsworth. Table LVI. shows these deaths arranged in monthly age periods. The heaviest mortality occurred at three to four months, while no deaths occurred at 10 to 11 months. The increase in the deaths, compared with previous years, was due to the increase in the number of deaths from Diarrhoea and Enteritis. It is a most significant fact that the number of deaths from Diarrhoea and Enteritis among 2,880 infants who were breastfed was only four, or a percentage mortality of "14, while among 638 infants who were otherwise fed the number of deaths was 82 or a percentage mortality of 13. The mortality among infants otherwise fed was thus 93 times greater than those breast-fed. The mortality among the infants visited by the Inspectors was only 43 per 1,000, compared with 122 per 1,000 the rate for the Borough for the year. 110 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The Chart shows the male and female as well as the total death-rate from Pulmonary Tuberculosis per 10,000 population for the last 25 years. As will be seen the rate in both males and females has decreased to some extent. The rate among males was lowest in 1910 and among females in 1902. In the Report for 1910 the methods which had been adopted by some authorities were given, but since then the subject of the control of Pulmonary Tuberculosis has been widely discussed, not only in medical journals, but also in the lay press. The education of the public which has been going on for some years has borne some fruit in the establishment of societies for dealing with the question. The medical profession in the Borough, as represented by the South West London Medical Society and the Wandsworth Division of the British Medical Association, formed a joint Committee which met several times for the purpose of formulating a scheme to lay before the Council. Two schemes were eventually formulated, in one of which the Local Authority were to have the control, and the other, a voluntary scheme controlled by a central body in London, and in which the Local Authority would as such have no control. The joint Committee favoured the former scheme, but both were printed and circulated to members of the Council. A deputation from the above mentioned bodies came before the Council to support the scheme, and eventually it was referred to the Public Health Committee. Previous to this the Public Health Committee had the matter under consideration, and the following report was made to the Council on the 26th July :— "(a) We have had under consideration the subject of the establishment of a dispensary for the treatment of patients suffering from Tuberculosis. We have considered the report of the Paddington Dispensary for the Prevention of Consumption, which was Death-rate per 100,000 from Tuberculosis of the Lungs from 1887 to 1911 inciusive. 119 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. opened in January, 1909, 877 persons being treated in the first year; the report of the Medical Officer of Health, Portsmouth, on Tuberculin dispensaries ; and the report of the Medical Officer of Health, London County Council, as to the action taken by the London Sanitary Authorities with regard to cases of Pulmonary Tuberculosis. "(b) In 1903 the Council adopted a system of voluntary notification of the disease; cases coming under the Poor Law authorities have been notified under an order of the Local Government Board since 1908, and the principle of compulsory notification of cases of Pulmonary Tuberculosis has, from the 1st of May, been extended to cases occurring amongst in-patients or out-patients at hospitals or other similar institutions for the treatment of the sick which are supported wholly or partially otherwise than by contributions of patients and otherwise than from rates and taxes. The Council, in March last, decided, in order that the matter might be more satisfactorily dealt with, to ask the London County Council to consider the advisability of adopting a system of compulsory notification of Tuberculosis within the County of London. (c) The total number of deaths from Tuberculosis in the Borough during the year 1910 was 264—49 in Clapham, 24 in Putney, 61 in Streatham, 35 in Tooting, and 95 in Wandsworth ; and the death-rate was .88 per 1,000—.84 in Clapham, .85 in Putney, .65 in Streatham, 109 in Tooting, and 108 in Wandsworth ;—the average for 10 years for the whole Borough (1900 to 1909) being .93. Although there has been a considerable decrease compared with the decennial average, yet we feel that further steps are necessary to lessen the mortality. "(d) At the present time over three hundred cases of Pulmonary Tuberculosis are under the observation of the Public Health Department. There are also over two hundred cases at present under treatment at the various Poor Law institutions, and the annual cost is estimated at about £6,000, without taking into consideration the amount expended in outdoor and other relief to the dependents. 120 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. "(e) We are strongly of opinion that something should be done with the view of assisting persons suffering from the disease. A Tuberculosis Dispensary, established by the Council, under the supervision of the Medical Officer of Health, on the lines of a similar institution recently established at Portsmouth, could be conducted at a cost of about £700 per annum ; and we understand that such an institution would receive the hearty support of the medical profession in the district. Patients in the early stages of Consumption could thus be treated while still following their occupations, and probably prevented from becoming chargeable to the rates. "(f) The Medical Officer of the Local Government Board, in a memorandum on the administrative measures against Tuberculosis, states with regard to the Tuberculosis Dispensary 'The object of this institution is to secure early diagnosis for patients suspected to be suffering from Pulmonary Tuberculosis, and to direct their treatment in the light of knowledge not only of their medical, but also of their domestic and industrial needs. The ideal of the dispensary implies, therefore, a careful system of domicilary visitation and investigation.' "(g) We submit the following statement of the estimated cost of furnishing and fitting up a dispensary, and of the annual cost of maintenance:— Furnishing, fitt ng-up, apparatus, etc. £120 0 0 Maintenance— £ s. d. Salaries—Medical Officers £300 00 Nurse 75 0 0 Caretaker 50 0 0 ———- 425 00 Rent 45 00 Rates, Taxes, Water, etc. 20 0 0 Coal and Gas 10 0 0 Drags, including Tuberculin 200 0 0 £700 0 0 121 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. "2.We recommend that a dispensary for the treatment of Tuberculous patients be established by the Council, and be under the control of the Medical Officer of Health." The above report was referred back to the Committee with the view of further information on the subject being supplied to members of the Council. This report was again brought before the Council on the 4th October, but it was again rendered back to the Committee for further consideration. On the instructions of the Committee the following report was made and sent to all members of the Council. After a recapitulation of the methods adopted by some Authorities which appeared in my Report for 1910 the report was as follows:— "II.—The Dispensary would be the centre where all the measures for the prevention and cure of the disease would be centralised. "III.—As has been stated by the late Medical Officer to the Local Government Board, in a preface to a report on Tuberculosis Sanatoria by Dr. Bulstrode, whatever be the precise value of sojourn in a sanatorium, there can be no question that the average of lasting benefit would be materially increased were a system of selection generally adopted for securing as inmates persons in the earlier phase of their malady. This want a Tuberculosis Dispensary would supply, but in addition it would enable the Authority to carry out more completely other measures for dealing with the disease. "IV.—The measures which are at present carried out are :— (1.) Notification. (2.) Visiting cases, leaving card of advice, and exercising a certain amount of supervision over those treated at home. 122 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. (3.) Cleansing and disinfection of premises after death or removal. (4.) The provision of spit bottles. (5.) The examination of sputum. "V.—The other measures which would be carried out if the Dispensary were started would be:— (1.) The treatment by Tuberculin. (2.) The examination of contacts discovered after notified cases. (3.) Treatment and visiting at home of those cases not able to attend at the Dispensary. (4.) Organisation of voluntary associations, who would assist in procuring extra food, clothing, etc. (5.) Arranging for removal to Sanatoria of suitable cases. (6.) The provision of outdoor shelters. (7.) More thorough education of contacts and suspects in the methods of prevention. "VI.—The two points in the scheme which are of the greatest importance are the searching for and examination of contacts, ..and the treatment by Tuberculin. "VII.—The following is a detailed statement of the estimated cost of furnishing and fitting up a Dispensary, and the annual cost of maintenance:— Furnishing and fitting up rooms— Tables, chairs, linoleum, gas or electric light fittings, laboratory bench, and sink £40 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 123 Apparatus—including microscope weighing machine, laryngoscope, clinical thermometers, steriliser, hypodermic syringes, centifuge, incubator, and other bacteriological apparatus £80 £120 Maintenance— Salaries— Medical Officers,—3 at £100 £300 (Each Medical Officer would be in attendance two afternoons each week, and would also visit selected cases at home.) Nurse, appointed as Sanitary Inspector and Health Visitor, at £120 to £150 per annum, part repayable by London County Council £75 Caretaker, also acting as porter and clerk (house, gas and coals provided) £50 Rent £45 Rates, Taxes, Water, etc. £20 Coals and gas £10 Drugs, including Tuberculin £200 £700 "VIII.—Duties of Medical Officers:— (1.) To attend at Dispensary at specified hours, for examination and treatment of cases. (2.) To visit selected cases at home unable to attend at Dispensary. (3.) To examine contacts sent to Dispensary. (4.) To make bacteriological examinations of sputum. (5.) To enter on forms details of examination and treatment. (6.) To prepare and forward fortnightly reports on prescribed forms to the Medical Officer of Health, for the information of the Council. 124 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. "[IX.—Duties of Nurse:— (1.) To attend daily at Public Health Department, to see the Medical Officer of Health, and receive instructions with reference to notified cases. (2.) To attend daily at Dispensary, to render assistance to doctors. (3.) To visit such cases as are necessary, to fill in report sheets, leave the literature issued by the Council, and to give instruction to the patient or relatives. (4.) To prepare and forward fortnightly reports on prescribed forms to the Medical Officer of Health for the information of the Council. (5.) To note and report on any insanitary conditions in the homes visited." 23rd September, 1911. The Public Health Committee after further consideration reported again to the Council on the 15th November as follows "(1.) We have further considered the subject of the proposed establishment of a dispensary for the treatment of persons suffering from Tuberculosis, and we have again had under consideration the Order of the Local Government Board, dated 22nd March, 1911, extending the compulsory notification of Pulmonary Tuberculosis to cases occurring amongst in-patients or out-patients at certain classes of hospitals or other similar institutions for the treatment of the sick. The letter of the Local Government Board accompanying the Order pointed out that Councils are empowered to provide hospitals for the treatment of Pulmonary Tuberculosis, and stated that the Board is advised that this power extends to the provision of dispensaries or hospitals for the treatment of outpatients. "(2.) After careful enquiry and full reconsideration of the proposals submitted to the Council on the 4th October, and after .reading the latest report on the dispensary which is now being carried on at Portsmouth, we have to report that in our judgment the scheme recommended by us is at the present time sufficient. Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 125 "(3.) With regard to the support which the proposed dispensary would receive from the medical profession, we have to report that the scheme recommended by us is in accordance with the views expressed by the deputation which waited upon the Council on the 26th July last. We also desire to point out that the dispensary would be entirely under the control of the Council. The medical profession have neither asked nor suggested that they should have any control over the scheme when in operation. "(4.) With regard to the cost of the proposed dispensary, having carefully reconsidered the subject, we submit the following amended estimate, in which some additions have been made to the amounts previously stated, viz. : Furnishing and fitting up rootns— Tables, chairs, linoleum, gas or electric light fittings, laboratory bench, and sink £40 Apparatus—including microscope, weighing machine, laryngoscope, clinical thermometers, steriliser, hypodermic syringes, centifuge, incubatoi\ and other bacteriological apparatus £80 £120 Maintenance— Salaries— Medical Officer (who would also act as Deputy Medical Officer of Health) £300 Xurse, appointed as Sanitary Inspector and Health Visitor £120 Caretaker, also acting as porter and clerk (house, gas, and coals provided) £65 Rent £60 Rates, Taxes, Water, etc £25 Coals and Gas £10 Drugs, including Tuberculin £200 £780 126 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. "(5-) With regard to the object of the proposed dispensary, we desire to state that it would not be solely a place where Tuberculin treatment is given, but would be the centre from which would radiate all the municipal measures in the Borough for the cure and prevention of consumption. "(6.) We beg to point out, however, that the scheme which we suggest is susceptible of extension, if the Council should so decide. "(7.) We also beg to report that if the Council should desire to add to the scheme the provision of beds in sanatoria, this t an be secured at Peppard or other sanatoria at a cost of 30s. per bed per week. "(8.) In conclusion we have to report that, after carefully reconsidering the whole subject, we endorse our previous report, and again recommend the Council to establish a dispensary, on the lines suggested, for the treatment of persons suffering from Tuberculosis, and that such dispensary be under the control of the Public Health Committee ; and that the matter be referred to us to take the necessary, steps to carry out." This report, after discussion by the Council, was withdrawn. Since the date of the last report I have again carefully studied the whole subject and I am decidedly of opinion that the establishment of a Tuberculosis Dispensary on the lines suggested by the Public Health Committee would be the most satisfactory means of dealing with the question, as far as our present knowledge goes. Many other Authorities have quite recently established dispensaries on similar lines and others are considering the question, and if the Council is to do anything in the way of checking Tuberculosis no more satisfactory or economical scheme could, in my opinion, be adopted. It may with some reason be argued that the death-rate from Tuberculosis in the Borough is less compared with the rest of the Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 127 Metropolis, but if the constitution of the population and the number of open spaces in the Borough be taken into consideration the rate should be lower than nearly all the other Metropolitan Boroughs. Again, the reduction in the mortality in the Borough, compared with the decennial average was only 7.4 per cent., while the reduction in the Metropolis was 13 per ccnt. In Edinburgh, according to the report of the Medical Officer of Health, the mortality had been reduced 45 per cent, during the last 20 years, this period corresponding almost exactly with the time the Tuberculosis Dispensary in that City has been in operation. The treatment by Tuberculin is now being tried in the majority of these dispensaries, but whether this will prove as satisfactory as the supporters of the treatment allege, cannot yet be definitely stated. As far as present experience goes the treatment is of great value in early cases, and I have from personal observation been able to verify this in a number of cases both in private practice and in dispensaries. Compared with London as a whole the mortality was "91 per 1,000 compared with 1.34, while the only other Boroughs with a lower rate were Hampstead with "61, and Lewisham with "64 per 1,000. Notification of Consumption under the Local Government Board's Order of December, 1908. 288 cases of Consumption were notified during the year, 42 of these belonging to Clapham, 16 to Putney, 41 to Streatham, 75 to Tooting, and 114 to Wandsworth, compared with 219 cases in iyio, and 317 in 1909. 128 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Two cases in Streatham and eight in Wandsworth were discharged to Common Lodging Houses. Notification of Cases under Tuberculosis in Hospitals Regulations, 1911. The above regulations came into force on the 1st May, 1911, and applied to all hospitals, dispensaries, or similar Institutions for the treatment of the sick which is partially or wholly supported otherwise than by the contributions of the persons treated therein or of their relatives or guardians, and otherwise than from rates and taxes. In the year 1909, 1910, and up to the 1st May, 1911, however, several notifications had been received from hospitals, chiefly the Brompton Hospital, but such notifications were purely voluntary. '/ , The whole of the work in connection with these cases has been carried out by the female Sanitary Inspectors, and I am able to report that it was done in a most thorough and satisfactory manner. The total number of visits paid was 938, 180 in Clapham, 54 in Putney, 178 in Streatham, 148 in Tooting, and 378 in Wandsworth. As in previous years, the pamphlet which has been in use since 1903 was left, and the instructions thereon were in most of the cases carried out. As there are no Public Institutions in the Borough to which consumptive patients can be sent, the Inspectors have referred children to the Invalid Children's Association, and in the majority of cases they have been able to obtain convalescent treatment for them. Where great poverty existed in the house the Invalid Children's Aid Association was unable to give prolonged assistance, and in those instances the matter had to be referred to the Guardians. Certain adult cases were referred to the Charity Organisation Society, which has helped in giving Hospital Letters and convalescent treatment. 136 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. On the 1st September, 1911, the above disease was made notifiable in London and since that date five cases were notified in this Borough, and 69 in the whole of London. Acute Polio-Myelitis has Becn for many years known under the name of Infantile Paralysis, and occurs chiefly among children. In recent years greater attention has Becn called to it on account of epidemics in America, Australia and Sweden. In this country localised outbreaks have occurred in Devon, Cornwall, and in the Midlands, and these outbreaks have Becn investigated by the Local Government Board. 150 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. In December, 1911, the Board circulated a Memorandum to Sanitary Authorities giving particulars of the symptoms of the disease, its fatality, and methods of control that should be adopted. As this Memorandum was of such importance, not only to Health Authorities, but also to Medical Practitioners, containing as it did information that could not be derived from ordinary text books, I advised the Council that it would be most desirable that every medical man in the Borough should possess a copy of it. The Council agreed to this and a copy was sent to every medical practitioner in the Borough. The following extract from this Memorandum with reference to the means to be adopted for the control of the disease was as follows:— "The possibility of administrative control over Polio-Myelitis is greatly increased by active co-operation between the medical officer of health and the medical practitioners in a district. In view of the difficulty in diagnosing certain forms of Polio-Myelitis, medical practitioners should be invited to confer with the medical officer of health as to cases of anomalous illness; thus the medical officer of health may be enabled to assist the practitioner with information as to similar cases which may be in his possession. "The Sanitary Authority should be prepared to give facilities for the examination by competent bacteriologists of material derived from the sick. "It is important that precautionary measures should be taken in all suspected cases. As it has Becn shown experimentally that the virus is present in the nasal mucous membrane, an antiseptic solution should be applied by means of a spray to the throats and nasal passages, not only of the patients, but of all persons brought into contact with them. The virus can be killed experimentally by a one per cent. solution of peroxide of hydrogen, or by a solution of permanganate of potassium—1:500. "All discharges from the patient as well as all articles which may be soiled by such discharges should be immediately disinfected. 151 Report of the Medical Officer of Health "The sick should be isolated from the healthy, and if appropriate isolation and nursing cannot be obtained at home the patient should be treated in an isolation hospital. The sick room and its contents should be disinfected at the end of the illness. "No child should be allowed to attend school from an infected family." Of the five cases notified none died, but four of the cases were permanently paralysed, the latest inquiries in the current year showing that complete recovery had not taken place. The mortality is about 12 per cent. as a rule, but a large proportion of the cases who recover are permanently paralysed. Ophthalmia Neonatorum. The above disease was made a notifiable one in London on the 1st March, 1911, and since then 34 cases have Becn notified in the Borough and 489 in the whole of London. This disease is, as its name implies, a disease of new born children. The importance of notification is that if treatment is not immediately carried out blindness results, and the principal duty of the Sanitary Authority is to see that the patients have immediate and proper treatment. When a notification has Becn received the Inspector visits the house and ascertains if a medical man is in attendance and if the mother has sufficient knowledge to carry out the treatment ordered. If not, then arrangements are made with district nurses to carry out the treatment, and if a doctor is not in attendance the parents are advised to call in one at once or take the patient to a special hospital. Of the 34 cases notified, six were in Clapham, one in Putney, 11 in Streatham, one in Tooting, and 15 in Wandsworth Three of the cases in Wandsworth occurred in the Workhouse. 152 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 31 of the cases were visited and it was ascertained that 14 were attended at birth by midwives, 15 were treated at hospitals as outpatients, two as in-patients, and three were removed to the Union Infirmary, the 11 remaining being treated at home. Two of the cases have lost the sight of one eye, one of these being still an out-patient at a hospital. The others, with the exception of two who died from other diseases, have, as far as could be ascertained, completely recovered. Glanders and Anthrax Orders. Under the above Orders two cases of Anthrax and five cases of Glanders were notified by the Veterinary Inspectors of the London County Council. In these cases the necessary steps in connection with the disinfection and cleansing of the premises were taken by the officials of the London County Council, but the officers of the Borough Council visited the premises and obtained the names of any men who had Becn in contact with the diseased animals and notified them that if any symptoms of illness appeared the fact should be at once reported. Chronic Poisonings. Two deaths occurred from the above, one from Lead Poisoning in the person of a house painter, aged 43, and the other, in which an Inquest was held, from Ptomaine Poisoning from eating tinned pineapple. In the latter case it was much to be regretted that no information was given before the death of the patient by the medical man in attendance, and it was not till after the Inquest was held that inquiries were made. Careful inquiry into the history of the case showed that in all probability the death was not due to so-called Ptomaine Poisoning, but to poisoning by tin. All the occupants of the house suffered from symptoms of poisoning and a personal inquiry into these symptoms strengthened the opinion I formed that tin was the cause. Unfortunately I was not able to procure any of the fruit for analysis as it had all Becn consumed and the tin itself destroyed. Samples were taken from shops in the vicinity with the result that in every one tin was found to be present, but not in excessive quantity. The following report was made to the Public Health Committee on the matter, and a copy of it forwarded to the Local Government Board:— "Report re Food Poisoning at Park Hill, Clapham. "On Friday, August 4th, my attention was called to a paragraph in the Daily Papers with reference to the death from so-called Ptomaine poisoning at Park Hill, Clapham, an inquest having Becn held on a lady who died at that adddress on the 29th July. No previous information had Becn given to me either by the Coroner, the medical man in attendance, or by the relations of the deceased. "My inquiries showed that all the occupiers of the house partook of tinned pineapple on the 26th July, and that all suffered from similar symptoms, viz.: colic, vomiting and diarrhoea. Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 157 "On inquiry I have ascertained that six people sat down to dinner on Wednesday night, the 26th, their ages being respectively, occupier of house 58 years, wife 54 years, a son 22 years, a daughter 19 years, one lady guest 19 years, and one male guest 30 years. The dinner consisted of soup made from stock, joint and sweets, which consisted of the pineapple in question and custard. The only dish partaken of by all the six and also by the servants was the pineapple. I also ascertained that the pineapple had Becn purchased on the Saturday previously by the wife of the occupier and that it was made into a jelly by the cook on Wednesday morning, the gelatine used being ordinary leaf gelatine, the stock of which was in the house, and part of which had Becn used with no ill results. The pineapple jelly after being made was kept in the larder from mid-day until it was placed on the table about 8.30 p.m. "The occupier's wife partook of the pineapple jelly only, she had no soup, joint or other sweet. She was the first to be affected, having Becn taken ill with vomiting, diarrhoea and colic about 3 a.m. The husband was taken ill about three hours afterwards and the daughter at the same time. The son is stated to have Becn ill about seven hours after partaking of it (i.e., about 4 a.m.), the female guest about 6 in the morning and the male guest, although he felt sick about an hour after dinner did not have any definite symptoms until the evening of the next day, nearly 24 hours after. "With regard to the servants I have Becn informed that the cook returned about 10 p.m. and finished the pineapple jelly. The housemaid who had taken some jelly previously was taken ill about 4 a.m. and the cook was taken ill about 5 a.m. "The symptoms in all the cases were the same, but the occupier's wife was exceedingly ill and in a state of collapse when the Doctor saw her early in the morning. "I have also seen the medical man in attendance who gave me the particulars of the illness and who informed me that he saw the tin in which the pineapple was bought, but that he could not see anything unusual. 158 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. "No analysis of the contents of the stomach was made, although if I had Becn present at the Inquest I should have advised the Coroner to have had this done. "Taking all the circumstances into consideration I think it much more likely to have Becn a case of chemical poisoning by tin rather than a toxin poison. "I am having several samples taken of tinned pineapple and will report to you the result in due course." Diseases of the Brain and Nervous System. From these diseases 358 deaths occurred, compared with 351 in 1910, 372 in 1909, and 320 in 1908. 75 of these deaths were in Clapham, 35 in Putney, 112 in Streatham, 39 in Tooting, and nine in Wandsworth. 74 of these deaths occurred in Clapham, 38 in Putney, 108 in Streatham, 29 in Tooting, and 113 in Wandsworth. 25 of the deaths were due to Meningitis, 147 to Cerebral Haemorrhage or Apoplexy, 29 to Convulsions, 22 to Softening of the Brain, 29 to Paralysis, 27 to General Paralysis of the Insane, seven to Locomotor Ataxy, 13 to Epilepsy, and 59 to other diseases of the Nervous System. Circulatory Diseases. From the above group of diseases 359 deaths occurred, 140 of which, 68 males and 72 females, were due to Organic disease of the Heart. 74 of these deaths occurred in Clapham, 38 in Putney, 105 in Streatham, 29 in Tooting, and 113 in Wandsworth. Of the deaths from all diseases of the Heart, three were from two to five years, 14 from five to 15 years, 12 from 15 to 25 years, 47 from 25 to 45 years, 103 from 45 to 65 years, and 180 over 65 years. The number of deaths in 1911 was 47 more than in 1910, but 43 under the corrected decennial average. Respiratory Diseases. The total number of deaths was 531, 263 of males and 268 of females, compared with 509 in 1910, 690 in 1909, 543 in 1908, 550 in 1907, and 511 in 1906. The number of deaths was 22 more than in the previous year, but 79 under the corrected decennial average. 117 of the deaths occurred in Clapham, 52 in Putney, 129 in Streatham, 58 in Tooting, and 175 in Wandsworth. Compared with 1910, there was a decrease in Streatham and Tooting, and an increase in Clapham and Wandsworth, while in Putney the number of deaths was the same. 225 of the deaths occurred from Bronchitis, compared with 240 in 1910; 29 from Lobar Pneumonia, 115 from Lobular or Broncho-Pneumonia, 130 from Pneumonia in which the exact form was not distinguished; and 32 from other diseases of the Respiratory Organs. 160 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 121 of the deaths were of infants under one year, 44 from one to two years, 36 from two to five years, 11 from five to 15 years, nine from 15 to 25 years, 34 from 25 to 45 years, 91 from 45 to 65 years, and 185 over 65 years. The greatest number of deaths from these diseases occurred among persons under one year and over 65 years. From Bronchitis alone 133 deaths occurred among persons over 65 years of age, compared with 138 in 1910. Appendicitis and Typhlitis. 24 deaths occurred from these diseases, 14 of males and 10 of females, equal to a rate of 77 per million, compared with 21 in 1910, and 19 in 1909. It is advisable to make a special note of this cause of death as it seems to show a tendency to increase. This increase may be due to greater precision in certification, many of the deaths previously registered as due to Peritonitis having Becn doubtless due to Appendicitis. The Registrar General in his Report for England and Wales for 1909, states that the recorded death-rate had increased from 38 per million in 1901 to 64 in 1909, while the rate from Peritonitis has decreased from nearly 51 to 17. There has again been a marked increase in the number of deaths in Institutions, and to show that the increase has been going on for some years, the following Table has been prepared. The comparative percentages for London are also shown, and it will be noted that in the 20 years 1890-1909 these increased in London from 24.0 to 42.0), with an average of 32.51, and in Wandsworth from I4.6 to 30.5, with an average of 20.21. In this Borough the percentage is lower than for the whole of London, and this is what might be expected in consequence of the social condition of the population, but in spite of this, it is rather startling to find that nearly 30 per cent, of the total deaths occurred in Institutions, and that for the year 1910 14.5 per cent, occurred in Workhouses and Workhouse Infirmaries. During the year 100 notices with respect to 103 premises were received from the Water Board stating that the supply had been withdrawn. 10 of the premises were in Clapham, 15 in Putney, 36 in Streatham, 20 in Tooting, and 22 in Wandsworth. 176 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The supply was withdrawn for non-payment of rates from 54 premises from 31 premises in consequence of defects, in 16 the premises were empty, and in two the supply was withdrawn by request. Compared with 1910, there has been an increase of 24 in the number of notices received, but a decrease of one in the number of premises affected. When a notice has been received that the water has been cut off, either for arrears or in consequence of defects, the Sanitary Inspector visits the premises, and if these have no water supply, serves an intimation notice under the Public Health (London) Act. If this notice is not complied with the matter is reported to the Public Health Committee, so that a statutory notice may be served. During the year several cases have had to be so reported, and the Public Health Committee have considered the whole question of the procedure adopted by the Water Board in cutting off the water and so making an inhabited house unfit for human habitation, instead of taking action under Section 33 of the Metropolitan Water Board (Charges) Act, 1907. The matter was reported to the Council, who communicated with the Metropolitan Water Board, suggesting that proceedings might be taken under Section 33 of this Act. The Metropolitan Water Board replied to the Council's letter to the effect that each case was carefully considered before instructions were issued to withdraw the supply, and that the Committee were satisfied that those instructions were requisite for the protection of the Board's revenue, and also stated that instructions of this nature are only issued when the Committee are satisfied that they are necessary to prevent'an abuse of the Board's desire to reduce to a minimum the practice of withdrawing supplies for nonpayment of the water rate, and that in the cases referred to by the Council every endeavour had been made to secure payment before the supply was withdrawn. 177 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Mortuaries. The Mortuaries in Clapham, Streatham, Tooting and Wandsworth have been visited several times during the year, and I am able to report that the By-laws and Regulations have been carried out in a satisfactory manner. With reference to the Wandsworth Mortuary the Public Health Committee have on several occasions had the question of providing a new mortuary for Wandsworth before them, but so far no decision as to the site has been arrived at. The Mortuary at Tooting has been closed, but a temporary one has been provided pending the erection of a new one in Wandsworth. In Clapham, 43 bodies were removed to the Mortuary, compared with 21 in 1910 ; in Streatham 55, compared with 56 ; and in Wandsworth 108, of which 54 were from Wandsworth, 33 from Putney, and 21 from Tooting—a total of 206 compared with 186 in 1910. No infectious cases were admitted to any of the Mortuaries during the year. General Sanitary Work in 1911. The following list shows the special inspectiors made by me during the year (1)—In consequence of several complaints several inspections were made of the River Graveney at Southcroft Road, as the complaints were that after heavy rain the river overflowed and caused a nuisance on the land adjoining. It was found that when the river subsided large stagnant pools were left, and these gave rise to offensive smells, which were a nuisance to persons residing in the neighbourhood. Noticcs were served on the owner of the land and the nuisance was abated. f 2 178 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. (2)—Complaints were also made with respect to the brook at the rear of the houses in Strathbrook Road, near Norbury, and an inspection of the brook showed that in the summer months it was a nuisance. As this was a boundary ditch, and as it seemed that the best way to abate the nuisance was to pipe the brook for a considerable portion of its course, the matter was left in the hands of the Borough Engineer. (3)—Special inspections were made on several occasions during the year of the new roads between Moyser Road and Southcroft Road, Streatham. Several of these were in such a condition as to be a nuisance, but steps have now been taken by the Council, and by the owners in one instance, to carry out the work necessary to abate the nuisance. (4)—A complaint was received from the London County Council with respect to piggeries in Merton Road in close proximity to Southfields School, and on inspection it was found that a large number of pigs were kept in sties within 40 yards of a road leading to the school premises. The matter was reported to the Public Health Committee, who, after full consideration of the facts adjourned the matter for three months so as to give the owner an opportunity of removing the pigs and sties outside the limit laid down by the Public Health (London) Act. An irspection after three months was made, and 1 was able to report that the pigs had been removed from the sties in question. (5)—On the 25th August one of the Sanitary Inspectors reported that a band of Hungarian gypsies had encamped on land in Garratt Lane, and on inspecting the premises he found that there were about 120 men, women and children occupying 17 tents which had been erected on the land. He also reported that the sanitary accommodation in the house adjoining was totally insufficient, and that the gypsies were filthy in their habits and had not the most elementary knowledge of sanitation. Notices were served on the owner of the land to provide sanitary accommodation, and also on the occupiers of the tents for the insanitary conditions. 179 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. I visited the encampment on the nth September, but the tents had been taken down and the tribe were removing out of the Borough. The owner of the land removed the whole of the offensive matter which had been left. Considerable difficulty was experienced in getting these people removed, but the owner of the land, as soon as he lealised what his responsibilities were, gave every assistance, and thus facilitated their departure. At the same time, another small band of the same tribe rented part of a house and land in Southfield. As there were only 14 adults and 16 children in this tribe, the sanitary accommodation in this house was sufficient. These gypsies also had no idea of sanitation, but eventually came to realise that it was necessary for them to alter their habits. The house was a large one, but several of the rooms were in a dirty condition and notices were served on the occupier, who rented the premises to the gypsies, and these notices were complied with. The Sanitary Inspector visited the premises three or four times a week, and I personally inspected the premises on six different occasions, as several complaints had been received from the occupiers of the houses adjoining. The complaints made were mainly of the noise produced by hammering, the work carried on by these gypsies being the repairing of copper utensils. Eventually, after a considerable amount of difficulty, the tribe were persuaded to leave, and the premises were vacated on the 23rd December. 180 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. (6)—As in previous years, special inspections were made of several localities in consequence of complaints of smells from road gullies and sewer ventilators. The number of complaints was greater than in the previous year, especially in Streatham; this having been due to the fact that the climatic conditions were favourable. In all 214 complaints were received, compared with 117 in 1910: 11 from Clapham North, 10 from Clapham South, 24 from Putney, 56 from Streatham, 22 from Balham, 21 from Tooting, 33 from Southfield, 12 from Fairfield, and 25 from Springfield Wards. All these complaints were forwarded to the Borough Engineer, who reported that in all the cases the necessary cleansing and flushing, or other work, had been carried out. (7)—Inspections were also made of several passages and mews, with the result that several have been satisfactorily dealt with, but there still remains some in which, owing to legal difficulties as to ownership, the necessary work has not been done. (a) Yard at rear of 581-591, Wandsworth Road. Entrance and part of the yard paved with granite setts. (b) Passage at rear of 99-115, Balham High Road, and 1-13, Bedford Hill has been concreted in a satisfactory manner. (c) Passage at rear of 74-86, Balham High Road has been properly paved with concrete. (d) Passage at rear of 238-258, Balham High Road has been repaired. (e) Land at rear of Blackshaw Road, Tooting, has been paved and drained, and is now in a satisfactory condition. (/) Passage at rear of Furzedown Market, Tooting, has now been satisfactorily paved and drained by the owner. (g) Passage at rear of 154-162, High Street, Putney, has been properly paved. i8i Report of the Medical Officer of Health. (h) Passage at rear of 115-133, Mitcham Road, Tooting, is being satisfactorily repaired, but owing to frost the work was not completed at the end of the year. (i) Passage at rear of 174-190, Garratt Lane, Wandsworth, has not been done, but the matter is now in the Solicitor's hands and proceedings against the owners are being taken. (k) Passage at rear of 102-116, Mitcham Lane, Streatham, has been properly gravelled and the nuisance abated. (8)—Special house-to-house inspections were made during the year in Young's Rents, Rowlands Cottages, Rashleigh Street, and Union Grove, Clapham ; Biggs Row, Weimar Street, and Brewhouse Lane, Putney ; Greyhound Square, Mill Lane, New Park Road, Foss Road and Hazelhurst Road, Streatham ; Graveney Road, Selkirk Road, Trevelyan Road, Aldis Mews, Rookstone Road, and Letchworth Street, Tooting ; and Point Pleasant, Warple Way, Warple Road, Armoury Yard, Frogmore, Lydclen Road, Lydden Grove, and Wardley Street, Wandsworth. These inspections were made chiefly on account of nuisances and damp conditions reported to me by the Sanitary Inspectors, and also to ascertain if any of these premises could be reported to the Council under the Housing, Town Planning, &c., Act. Including the house-to-house inspections and inspections for certificates under the Revenue Act, but excluding the routine inspection of bakehouses, slaughter-houses, cowsheds, and offensive trade premises, 1,150 inspections were made of premises, chiefly previous to the service of statutory notices. My attendance at the Police Court was necessary on 10 occasions during the year (9)—A special inspection was also made in company with the Borough Engineer of the land at Putney Vale acquired by the Council for the extension of the Cemetery there, and the following report was made:— 182 Report of the Mcdical Officer of Health. " Putney Vale Cemetery Extension. "I have made an inspection of the land proposed to be acquired by the Council at Putney Vale for an extension of the Cemetery, and have to report as follows :— "This land consists of about 30 acres, part of which is arable and part grazing land. The highest point of the land, which is close to Wimbledon Common, is about no feet above ordnance datum, and the lowest point, which is close to Newlands Farm, is about 45 feet. "The soil is a mixture of mould, loam and clay, the latter differing in character in the trial holes, in some being more friable and containing a larger admixture of sand than in others. Generally speaking, the geological conditions are very similar to those in the present cemetery. "Six trial holes have been dug at different points to the depth of 8 feet, and in four of these no water was found at the time, but three days after water, varying in depth from six to to 23 inches, had collected. In other two a few inches of water were found at the time of opening, and after three days this had increased to two feet in one and four feet in the other. In these two the soil was of a more pervious character. "The ground water has a natural flow to the Beverley Brook, which is about 500 yards distant. "There will be 110 difficulty in draining the whole of the land to the depth necessary to render the graves dry, as there is a sewer of sufficient capacity in close proximity. "There are no sources of domestic water supply near the site, all the houses in the neighbourhood being supplied with water by the Metropolitan Water Board. "In my opinion, with proper drainage the proposed site is suitable for a cemetery." I83 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Revenue Act. During the year the Council considered the question of the Regulations as to underground rooms habitually used as sleeping places, and the following were drawn up and approved by the Local Government Board on the 26th May, 1911. " REGULATIONS AS TO UNDERGROUND ROOMS HABITUALLY USED AS SLEEPING PLACES. " Whereas by Section 17 of the Housing, Town Planning, &c., Act, 1909, it is enacted as follows :— " Sub-Section 7. A room habitually used as a sleeping place, the surface of the floor of which is more than three feet below the surface of the part of the street adjoining, or nearest to the room shall for the purposes of this section be deemed to be a dwelling house so dangerous or injurious to health as to be unfit for human habitation, if the room either— "(a) Is not on an average at least seven feet in height from floor to ceiling ; or "(b) Does not comply with such regulations as the local authority, with the consent of the Local Government Board, may prescribe for securing the proper ventilation and lighting of such rooms, and the protection thereof against dampness, effluvia, or exhalation. Provided that if the local authority, after being required to do so by the Local Government Board, fail to make such regulations, or such regulations as the Board approve, the Board may themselves make them, and the regulations so made shall have effect as if they had been made by the local authority, with the consent of the Board. "Provided that a closing order made in respect of a room to which this sub-section applies shall not prevent the room being used for purposes other than those of a sleeping place ; 185 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. and that, if the occupier of the room after notice of an order has been served upon him fails to comply with the order, an order to comply therewith may, on summary conviction, he made against him. "Now therefore we, the Mayor, Aldermen and Councillors of the Metropolitan Borough of Wandsworth being the local authority for the said Metropolitan Borough, hereby prescribe the following Regulations with which a room habitually used as a sleeping place, the surface of the floor of which is more than three feet below the surface of the part •of the street adjoining or nearest to the room, shall comply, namely: — " (a) Such room shall in every part thereof have at least three feet of its height above the level of the surface of the street or ground adjoining, or nearest to such room. Provided that if the width of the area hereinafter mentioned is not less than the height of the room from the floor to the said surface, or is not less than six feet, the height of the room above such surface may be less than three feet but not in any case less than one foot. " (b) Every wall of such room shall be constructed with a proper horizontal damp-proof course, and, if in contact with the soil, shall be effectually protected against dampness from that soil by means of a vertical dampproof course, or otherwise. " (c) There shall be outside of and adjoining such room and extending along the entire frontage thereof and open upwards from six irche? below the level of the floor thereof an area properly paved and effectually drained and at least four feet wide in every part ; provided that the width of such area may be not less in an\T part than three feet, if the mean width of the area be not less than four feet ; provided also that in the area there may be placed steps necessary for access to the room, and over and across such area there may be steps necessary for access to any buildings above the room, if the steps are so placed in such case as not to be over or across any external window of the room. 186 Report oj the Medical Officer of Health. "The space, if any, beneath the floor of such room shall be provided with adequate means of ventilation. "(e) Every drain passing under any such room shall be properly constructed of a gas-tight pipe. "(/) Such room shall be effectually protected against the rising of any effluvia or exhalation by means of a layer of good cement concrete at least six inches thick laid upon the soil of the site of the entire room or in some other equally effectual manner. "(g) Every such room which is without a fireplace,, and a flue properly constructed and properly connected with such fireplace, shall be provided with special and adequate means of ventilation by a sufficient aperture or air shaft, which shall provide an unobstructed sectional area of 100 square inches at the least. "(h) Such room shall have one or more windows opening directly into the external air, with a total area clear of the sash frames equal to at least one-tenth of the floor area of the room, and so constructed that one half at least of every such window can be opened, and that the opening may in every case extend to the top of the window. "The Common Seal of the Council was by order of the Council affixed this Third day of May, iqn, in the presence of HEX. GEO. HILLS, Town Clerk " The consent of the Local Government Board is hereby given to the foregoing Regulations this Nineteenth day of May, 191 x. H. C. MONRO, Secretary. "Acting on behalf of the said Board under the authority of their General Order, dated the Twenty-sixth day of May, 1877." 187 Report of the Medical Officer of Health At the present time inspections are being made to ascertain the number of rooms to which these Regulations would apply but so far very few have been discovered, and in many of these the occupation as a sleeping room has been discontinued as the occupier had other available rooms in the house. I have personally inspected most of the cases reported by the Sanitary Inspectors, but in none of those visited were the infringements of the Regulations of a serious character. In this Borough very few of the houses erected in recent years have basements, and it is chiefly in houses which have been made down so as to become tenement houses that there is any separate letting of basements. I shall be able to lay before the Public Health Committee early in the present year a report of the inspection of the Borough in this respect and also the result of any action taken. Sanitary Operations. The Table of Sanitary operations shows in detail the amount and nature of the work carried out by the Sanitary Inspectors during the year. The number of complaints received and attended to was 2,076, compared with 1789, in 1910, 1,692 in 1909, 1,645 in 1908, and 1,836 in 1907. There was an increase in Clapham, North and South, Putney, Streatham, Balham, Tooting and Springfield Wards, and a decrease in Southfield Ward, while Fairfield Ward is the same, compared with the previous year. Seven of these complaints were made to the London County Council, and these were at once attended to. 188 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The number of houses and premises inspected was 31,973, an increase of 2,042 compared with 1910, and of 5,038 compared with 1909. There was an increase in the number of houses and premises inspected in Clapham North and South, Streatham, Balham, Tootirg, Fairfield and Springfield Wards., and a decrease in Putney and Southfield Wards. The total number of house-to-house inspections made was 2,486, compared with 6,276 in 1910, 7,007 in 1909, 5,575 in 1908, 5,718 in 1907, and 5,686 in 1906. There has been an increase in the number of these inspections in Fairfield Ward, and a decrease in Clapham North and South, Putney, Streatham, Balham, Tooting, Southfield and Springfield Wards. Although the number of these inspections is much lower than in the five previous years, yet it will be noted that the total number of inspections has increased by 2,042. There were several reasons why the number of house-to-house inspections were lower, one being that the system adopted in these inspections and for recording the same has, as stated in the Report for last year, been altered so as to comply with the Housing, Town Planning, &c., Act, 1909. Another reason was that a greater number of inspections was made after notification of infectious diseases, as well as after Measles, Whooping Cough and other diseases notified from Elementary Schools. A third reason was that many inspections were made of premises after notification from the Education Department of the London County Council of verminous children to ascertain if it was necessary to deal with the premises under the London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1904. 189 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The total number of intimation notices served was 6,278, compared with 8,866 in 1910, and 9,336 in 1909, and of statutory notices 957, compared with 1,038 and 1,119. The greatest number of intimation notices was served in Clapham North and Springfield Wards, and the smallest number in Southfield and Putney Wards. The greatest number of statutory notices was served in Balham and Springfield Wards, and the smallest number in Putney and Tooting Wards. 246 of the statutory notices, 13 in Clapham, two in Putney, 36 in Streatham, three in Tooting, and 192 in Wandsworth, served in 1911, were not complied with at the close of the year. 54 of these notices were complied with in the beginning of the current year, 15 were for nuisance in a passageway in which Police Court proceedings are pending, 117 were for 50 premises in Wandsworth, and the remainder are in hand or the premises are empty. In the 50 premises in Wandsworth for which 117 notices were served, the work in some of the premises is in hand and the owner intends to deal with the rest shortly. Summonses have been issued for non-compliance with these notices, and at present as the work is in hand the hearing of these summonses has been adjourned. The number of statutory notices served include By-law notices, 305 of which were served in respect of the following contraventiors of the By-laws :—Yard paving I39> repairs to water closets 49, repairs to flushing apparatus of water closets 29, cleansing of water closet one, covers to cisterns 21, cleansing cisterns 12, cleansing house and other contraventions of the Bylaws as to houses let in lodgings 36, absence of cover to manure receptacle one, failing to deposit manure in a proper receptacle 15, absence of water closet accommodation to tents and vans one, and offensive liquid running over pavement one. 203 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The number of cases of overcrowding dealt with during the year was 91, compared with i2q in iqio, 131 in iqoq, 158 in 1908, 87 in 1907, 100 in iqo6, and 92 in 1905. Five of these were in Clapham, eight in Putney, 19 in Streatham, six in Tooting, and 53 in Wandsworth. The cases of overcrowding in Clapham were in Crichton Street, St. Andrew's Street, Midmoor Road, and Park Place ; in Putney, in Quill Lane, Sefton Street, Isabella Place, The Piatt, Modder Place, Mascotte Road, and March's Place ; in Streatham, in Larch Road, Zennor Road, Chestnut Grove, Wimbledon Road, Upper Tooting Road, Graveney Road, Besley Street, Westcote Road, Mill Lane, and Sulina Road ; in Tooting, in Tooting Grove, Ivellino Street, Gassiott Road, and Sellincourt Road : and in Wandsworth, in Bendon Valley, Lydden Road, Wandley Street, Abovne Road, Beechcroft Road, Thornsett Road, Groton Road, Garratt Lane, Warple Way, and Jews Row. Nearly all these were cases of domestic overcrowding and were remedied without difficulty by re-arrangement of the sleeping accommodation. 831 ashpits were provided or repaired, compared with 1,060 in 1910, 1,292 in 1909, and 329 complaints with reference to the non-removal of house refuse (60 from Clapham, 41 from Putney, xoo from Streatham, 15 from Tooting, and 113 from Wandsworth), were received during the year. These complaints were, as in previous years, attended to by the Dusting Inspectors. 1,105 yards in connection with dwelling-houses, and 123 in connection with stables, were paved, drained, or cleansed, compared with 1,437 and 97 in 1910. 5,026 rooms and 936 staircases and passages were cleansed or repaired, compared with 6,010 rooms and 1,266 staircases and passages in 1910. 204 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. The greatest number of rooms cleansed was in the Balham Ward, where 1,200 were done, and in Springfield Ward, where 727 were done, and the smallest number in Southfield and Clapham South, where the numbers were 230 and 344 respectively. 1,272 drains were cleansed or repaired, and 63 rain-water pipes disconnected from the drains. 158 notices of reconstruction of drains were sent to the Borough Engineer from this Department: 40 in Clapham, compared with 37 in 1910 ; nine in Putney, compared with seven; 64 in Streatham, compared with 36; 12 in Tooting, compared with 13; and 33 in Wandsworth, compared with 21. The total number of houses where the drains were reconstructed during the year was 349: 88 in Clapham, 20 in Putney,. 118 in Streatham, 25 in Tooting, and 98 in Wandsworth. 384 accumulations of manure were removed after service of notice, compared with 361 in 1910: 52 in Clapham, 69 in Putney, 145 in Streatham, 42 in Tooting, and 76 in Wandsworth. There has been an increase in the number of cases dealt with in Putney, Streatham and Tooting, and a decrease in Clapham and Wandsworth. 12 notices were served for the periodical removal of manure every 48 hours, compared with 38 in 1910 and 50 in 1909. The number of waterclosets cleansed and repaired was 1,909, compared with 2,149 in 1910. The greatest number of these were in Balham and Springfield Wards, and the smallest in Putney and Southfield Wards. 525 drinking water cisterns were cleansed, covered, or repaired: 88 in Clapham, 89 in Putney, 102 in Streatham, 66 in Tooting, and 180 in Wandsworth, compared with 606 in 1910. 205 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 107 premises were inspected and notices served where animals "were kept in such a manner as to be a nuisance. Four of these were in Clapham, three in Putney, 27 in Streatham, eight in Tooting, and 65 in Wandsworth. This nuisance was most common in Fairfield and Springfield Wards, and the animals kept were chiefly ducks and hens. 34 smoke nuisances were reported during the year: none in Clapham, four in Putney, one in Streatham, five in Tooting, and 24 in Wandsworth. Two complaints of black smoke were received from the Public Control Department of the London County Council with regard to two premises, and five complaints from the Coal Smoke Abatement Society with regard to five premises. These complaints were all at once attended to and the premises kept under observation. In one instance complaint was made to the Local Government Board by residents in Bendon Valley, Wandsworth, of nuisance from a laundry shaft in the vicinity and the following report was made on the matter :— "With reference to the letter from the Local Government Board, enclosing copy of a petition from 20 residents of Bendon Valley, Wandsworth, I have to report that the premises in question, viz.: The Primrose Laundry, has been under the observation of the Public Health Department for some time. On two occasions, in July, 1906, and again in October, 1907, proceedings were taken on the instructions of the Public Health Committee, but in neither case was a fine inflicted, the Magistrate making an order for the abatement of the nuisance with 6s. costs in each case. After the last conviction several alterations were made in the laundry and a new furnace and 206 Report of the Medical Officer of Healih. boiler fitted. In April, 1908, notice was served for black smoke for three minutes, but the Solicitor advised that no proceedings could be taken under the old order. During the rest of 1908, in 1909 and 1910, although observations were frequently made by the Inspector, only on one or two occasions was it reported that black smoke was issuing, and that only for a period of one or two minutes. In 1911 on two occasions in January black smoke was noticed for one minute, but since then although observation has been kept, no nuisance has been detected. "I have visited the premises and at the time of my visit I asked the stoker to stoke up in my presence, but this was not followed by any emission of black smoke. The furnaces are fitted with forced draught driven by a fan and in my opinion if properly used there should be no emission of black smoke. It is quite possible, however, that careless stoking would produce black smoke, and owing to the proximity of the furnace chimney to the houses in Benden Valley it is quite possible that on some occasions the nuisance noted by the complainants existed. "I have instructed the Inspector to take particular note of this chimney and if any black smoke is noted the matter will be reported to the Committee." In all, 189 observations for black smoke were made by the Sanitary Inspectors. 34 cases were repoited, and 31 notices were served. 194 soil pipes were repaired, 40 in Clapham, 16 in Putney, 55 in Streatham, 47 in Tooting, and 36 in Wandsworth, compared with 153 in 1910, and 182 in 1909. 869 leaky roofs and gutters were repaired, compared with 1,225 in 1910. and 1,159 in 1909. 207 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 30 unwholesome or dilapidated houses were cleansed and repaired, one in Clapham, none in Putney, two in Streatham, five in Tooting and 22 in Wandsworth, compared with 38 in 1910, and 46 in 1909. A large amount of work has again been carried out to abate nuisances arising from damp conditions. In all 1,228 premises have been dealt with, 259 in Clapham, 54 in Putney, 301 in Streatham, 160 in Tooting, and 453 in Wandsworth ; and in 444 of these, 86 in Clapham, one in Putney, 46 in Streatham, 60 in Tooting, and 250 in Wandsworth, damp courses have been inserted. I have personally inspected all the premises where damp courses were inserted as well as a large number of premises where this had been done in 1909 and 1910, and I am able to report that the dampness has been quite removed and the houses rendered much healthier. The number of unclassified nuisances was 765, 295 in Clapham, 49 in Putney, 216 in Streatham, 47 in Tooting, and 158 in Wandsworth, compared with 746 in 1910. Disinfection. The total number of rooms disinfected after the occurrence of cases of infectious disease was 4,419 (773 in Clapham, 237 in Putney, 1,261 in Streatham, 533 in Tooting, and 1,615 in Wandsworth), compared with 2,166 in 1910, 3,146 in 1909, 2,868 in 1908, 3,206 in 1907, and 2,794 in 1906. The increase was altogether due to the increase in the number of disinfections carried out after Measles, Scarlet Fever and Diphtheria. 208 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 1,603 lots bedding were stoved at the Disinfecting Station, compared with 1,230 in 1910, 1,584 in 1909. 1,826 in 1908, 1,948 in 1907, and 1,669 in 1906 The 1,603 lots of bedding included the following :—1,008 beds, 1,084 bolsters, 2,695 pillows, 1,187 mattresses, 433 palliasses, 1,952 sheets, 1,479 quilts, 686 cushions, 5,581 articles of clothing, 2,734 blankets, 146 carpets, and 2,628 extra articles, a total of 21,613 articles, compared with 15,430 in 1910, 20,843 in 1909, 24,034 in 7908, 29,102 in 1907, and 28,885 in 1906. 2,638 premises were disinfected after Measles and 313 after Tuberculous disease of the Lungs compared with 418 and 265 in 1910. 35 beds, 12 bolsters, 53 pillows, 46 mattresses, 38 palliasses, 13 sheets, 3 quilts, 18 cushions, 122 articles of clothing, 9 blankets, 31 carpets, and 87 extra articles, were removed and destroyed by request of the owners during the year. Disinfection was also carried out by the Sanitary Staff at premises after non-notifiable diseases, chiefly Cancer, and the sum of £31 7s. 6d. was received for such disinfection. 240 rooms were disinfected with sulphur in consequence of their verminous condition, and 11 lots of verminous bedding disinfected. I have regularly visited the Disinfecting Station during the year, and am able to report that the work has been carried out by the staff to my entire satisfaction. No complaints of damage to goods by disinfection have been received during the year. 209 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. Cost per lot of bedding 14s. 9d. Cleansing of Persons Act. The baths at the Disinfecting Station have been more frequently used this year, chiefly owing to the greater number of verminous children dealt with. The names of 361 children, 60 belonging to Clapham, 18 to Putney, 45 to Streatham, 40 to Tooting, and 198 to Wandsworth, stated to be in a verminous condition, were forwarded to me by the Medical Officer, Education Department, London County Council, and the homes were visited by the Inspectors. In the majority of cases only the heads of the children were verminous, but in a few cases the bodies and clothing were also verminous. Of these latter, 14, nine boys and five girls, were cleansed at the baths, and the clothing was at the same time stoved with beneficial results. 210 Report of the Medical Officer of Health. 18 adults also had baths, four being nurses who had been in attendance on cases of Puerperal Fever, nine were contacts with cases of Scarlet Fever or Diphtheria, while five, three females and two males, were verminous.
